Commentary
This passage is a definitional commentary on the topic of contradiction (tanaqud) in categorical propositions. The author begins by giving examples of contradictory pairs: a universal affirmative 'Every human is an animal by necessity' and a particular negative 'Some human is not an animal by general possibility', as well as a perpetual universal affirmative 'Every celestial sphere is in motion perpetually' and a particular negative absolute 'Some celestial sphere is not in motion absolutely'. These examples illustrate the general conditions. The author then explains the matn's statement 'its contradiction in quality is to change it', parsing the grammar to show that 'that' and the verb form a verbal noun substituting for 'quality', meaning the contradiction occurs by changing the quality. Next, the matn's 'and contradict with the opposite of its quantifier' is explained: the 'ba' means 'with', so one must bring the negation while varying the quantifier (i.e., universal to particular or vice versa). From this, two conditions are derived: changing the quality (from affirmative to negative or vice versa) and changing the quantity (from universal to particular or vice versa), while maintaining unity of subject, predicate, and relation. The author notes an exception in poetry where the 'fa' is omitted for metrical reasons. Then the author turns to the example 'Every human is an animal by necessity' to classify it as an absolute necessary proposition (daruriyya mutlaqa), contrasting it with the generally possible (mumkina 'amma). The necessary proposition asserts necessary predication as long as the subject exists; the generally possible asserts non-impossibility without further determination. The contradiction between them involves the universality/particularity, necessity/possibility, and positive/negative quality. The author then explains the truth conditions: if the mind allows negation of the predicate from some subject individuals, the particular negative is true; otherwise the universal affirmative is true. Next, the perpetual (da'ima) and general absolute (mutlaqa 'amma) are defined: the perpetual asserts eternal predication as long as the subject exists; the absolute asserts actual predication without temporal modal restriction. Finally, a poetic license example from Hassan ibn Thabit shows the omission of the 'fa' in conditional construction for poetic necessity. Overall, the passage establishes the precise rules for forming contradictory propositions in categorical logic.

Contradiction in Conditional Propositions: Genus and Species Conditions
Author extends contradiction rules to conditionals with genus/species conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct contradictory of any conditional proposition by applying the conditions of genus (conjunctive/disjunctive) and species (e.g., implicational/accidental).

Core Idea
To contradict a conditional, you must keep the genus and species—only flip quantity and quality.

In the previous lesson, we learned the conditions for contradiction in categorical propositions. Now the author extends this to conditional propositions (shartiyya). However, conditionals have an extra layer: they come in different genera (conjunctive vs disjunctive) and species (such as implicational, accidental, etc.). To find the contradictory, you must maintain the same genus and species while changing the quantity (universal↔particular) and quality (affirmative↔negative). This lesson will teach you to apply the expanded contradiction rules to any conditional proposition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus of the given conditional
Determine whether it is conjunctive (muttasila) or disjunctive (munfasila). Conjunctive conditionals express an implication ('if...then'), while disjunctive express alternatives ('either...or').
2
Identify the species
For conjunctive, determine if it is luzumiyya (implicational, necessary connection) or ittifaqiyya (accidental). For disjunctive, distinguish among 'inadiyya (contradictory opposition), haqiqiyya (true exclusive disjunction), mani'at al-jam' (cannot both be true), or mani'at al-khuluw (cannot both be false).
The species condition ensures that the contradictory is of the same logical subtype.
3
Determine the quantity and quality
Assess whether the proposition is universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative. In conditionals, 'universal' corresponds to 'always' or 'every time', and 'particular' corresponds to 'sometimes' or 'not always'.
4
Apply the contradiction rule
Change the quantity (universal to particular or vice versa) and quality (affirmative to negative or vice versa) while keeping the genus and species unchanged. For example, the contradictory of a universal affirmative implicational conjunctive is a particular negative implicational conjunctive.
The author summarizes: 'نقيض الكلية الموجبة اللزومية جزئية سالبة لزومية'
5
Verify with examples
Check your result using the examples provided in the passage. For instance, the contradictory of 'كلما كان هذا إنساناً، كان حيواناً' (universal affirmative conjunctive implicational) is 'قد لا يكون إذا كان إنساناً كان حيواناً' (particular negative conjunctive implicational).
Ensure that the genus and species match exactly.

The summary: a conditional is contradicted by another conditional that differs from it in quantity and quality, and agrees with it in genus and species. So the contradictory of a universal affirmative implicational [conditional] is a particular negative implicational, and the contradictory of a universal negative implicational is a particular affirmative implicational, and the accidental follows the implicational in that. The contradictory of a universal affirmative contradictory [disjunctive] is a particular negative contradictory, and analogize accordingly. His statement: (And examples of that are clear from what preceded), for example our saying: 'Whenever this is a human, it is an animal' its contradictory: 'It is not the case that whenever it is a human, it is an animal'; and our saying: 'It is absolutely not the case that whenever this is a human, it is a stone' its contradictory: 'It may be that whenever it is a human, it is a stone', and so on in both. And the contradictory of the unrestricted [conditional] is the contradictory of the particular. And our saying: 'Always either the number is even or it is odd' its contradictory: 'It is not always the case that either the number is even or it is odd'. And our saying: 'It is absolutely not the case that either the thing is a human or it is rational' its contradictory: 'It may be that either... etc.', and the unrestricted is like the particular.

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of contradiction but focuses on conditional propositions (shartiyya). It opens with the conclusion of the Quranic example from the previous page: the verse 'Who sent down the Scripture that Moses brought?', which contradicts the Jewish claim 'Allah has not sent down anything to any human' by converting the universal negative into a particular affirmative. The author notes this was stated by Ibn Harun. Then a caution (tanbih) is given: all the previously mentioned rules for contradiction (definition, conditions) apply to both categorical and conditional propositions, but for conditionals an additional condition must be added: unity in genus (jins) and species (naw'). In conditionals, genus refers to whether the proposition is conjunctive (muttasila) or disjunctive (munfasila). Species refers to subtypes: for conjunctive, whether it is implicational (luzumiyya) or accidental (ittifaqiyya); for disjunctive, whether it is contradictory ('inadiyya), accidental, true exclusive (haqiqiyya), preventing collection (mani'at jam'), or preventing emptiness (mani'at khuluw). The author then explains that in conditionals, the conditions of contradiction are similar but adapted: they must share the same genus and species, differ in quantity and quality, but not in modality (jiha) because conditionals do not admit modality in the same way. The summary gives specific rules: the contradictory of a universal affirmative implicational conditional is a particular negative implicational of the same species; for the universal negative implicational, the contradictory is a particular affirmative implicational. Accidental conditionals follow the pattern of implicational ones. For disjunctives, the contradictory of a universal affirmative 'inadiyya (contradictory disjunction) is a particular negative 'inadiyya. The author then provides examples: for the conjunctive 'Whenever this is a human, it is an animal', the contradictory is 'It is not always the case that when it is a human, it is an animal' (a particular negative implicational). For the absolute negative conjunctive 'It is absolutely not the case that whenever this is a human, it is a stone', the contradictory is 'It may be that when it is a human, it is a stone'. For disjunctives: 'Always either the number is even or it is odd' (a universal affirmative exclusive disjunction?) has the contradictory 'It is not always the case that either ...'. And 'It is absolutely not the case that either the thing is a human or it is rational' has the contradictory 'It may be that either ...'. The unrestricted (muhmala) conditionals follow the pattern of particular ones. This passage thoroughly extends the doctrine of contradiction to the full range of conditional propositions.

Conditions for Contradiction in Conditional Propositions
Bannani expands the rules of contradiction to conditionals with extra conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the additional conditions required for contradiction in conditional propositions and apply them to examples.

Core Idea
Contradiction in conditionals requires agreement in the type of connection, not just form.

In categorical propositions, contradiction requires difference in quantity and quality with unity of subject, predicate, etc. In conditionals, we need an extra layer: the two propositions must agree in their genus (conjunctive/disjunctive) and species (implicative/coincidental/adversative etc.). This ensures that the contradiction is genuine and not merely apparent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditional propositions
Locate the two conditionals being compared. Make sure both are fully stated (antecedent and consequent for conjunctives; disjuncts for disjunctives).
2
Check standard conditions
Verify that the two propositions have the same antecedent and consequent (or disjuncts), same time, same place, etc. These conditions apply to all contradictions.
If these differ, the pair cannot be contradictories.
3
Check genus unity
Ensure both propositions are either conjunctive (متصل) or both disjunctive (منفصل). They cannot be cross-type (one conjunctive, one disjunctive).
Genus here refers to conjunction vs disjunction.
4
Check species unity
For conjunctives, both must be either implicative (لزومية) or both coincidental (اتفاقية). For disjunctives, both must be the same subtype: adversative (عنادية), coincidental, genuine (حقيقية), exclusive-of-combination (مانعة جمع), or exclusive-of-void (مانعة خلوص).
If the type of connection differs, they are not genuine contradictories.
5
Verify quantity and quality difference
Finally, the two propositions must differ in quantity (universal vs particular) and quality (affirmative vs negative). For conditionals, universal is 'always' or 'never'; particular is 'sometimes' or 'sometimes not'.
Indefinite propositions (without quantifier) are treated like particulars.

تنبية: جميع ما ذكره يجري في الحملية وفي الشرطية، ويزاد في الشرطية شرط الاتحاد ففي الجنس، وهو الاتصال والانفصال، وفي النوع وهو في المتصلة كونها لزومية أو اتفاقية، وفي المنفصلة كونها عنادية أو اتفاقية، حقيقية، أو مانعة جمع، أو مانعة خلوص، وأمثلة ذلك واضحة مما تقدم . قوله : (فناقض السلب الكلي بالإيجاب الجزئي) أي : لأن قولهم : وما أنزل الله على بشر من شيء [الأنعام: 91] في قوة قولهم : لا شيء من البشر بمنزل عليه الكتاب»، وقوله تعالى : ﴿من أنزل الكتاب الذي جاء به موسى﴾ [الأنعام: 91] في قوة قوله : «بعض البشر أنزل عليه الكتاب»، وهذه جزئية موجبة صادقة تبطل كليتهم السالبة الكاذبة . قوله : ( جميع ما ذكره أي : من تعريف التناقض والشروط ؛ إلا أنه يعتبر في الشرطية ما تقبله من الشروط كالاتحاد في الطرفين والزمان والمكان، ونحو ذلك ؛ كاختلاف الكم لا الجهة؛ إذ لا تقبلها . وحاصله : أن الشرطية تناقضها شرطية أخرى تخالفها في كمها وكيفها، وتوافقها في جنسها ونوعها، فنقيض الكلية الموجبة اللزومية جزئية سالبة لزومية، ونقيض الكلية السالبة اللزومية الجزئية الموجبة اللزومية، والاتفاقية تابعة للزومية في ذلك، ونقيض الكلية الموجبة العنادية جزئية سالبة عنادية، وعلى هذا فقس. قوله : ( وأمثلة ذلك واضحة مما تقدم ) مثلاً قولنا : كلما كان هذا إنساناً، كان حيواناً نقيضه: قد لا يكون إذا كان إنساناً كان حيواناً»، وقولنا : «ليس البتة إذا كان هذا إنساناً، كان حجراً نقيضه : قد يكون إذا كان إنساناً كان حجراً ، وبالعكس فيهما ، ونقيض المهملة نقيض الجزئية. وقولنا : دائماً إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً، وإما أن يكون فرداً نقيضه : قد لا يكون إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً، وإما أن يكون فرداً». وقولنا : «ليس البتة إما أن يكون الشيء إنساناً، وإما أن يكون ناطقاً» نقيضه: «قد يكون إما أن يكون ... إلخ»، والمهملة كالجزئية.
Note: All that he mentioned applies to categorical and conditional [propositions]. And in the conditional, an additional condition of unity is added: in the genus, which is [the unity of] conjunction and disjunction; and in the species, which is in the conjunctive [conditional] being implicative or coincidental, and in the disjunctive being adversative or coincidental, genuine, or exclusive-of-combination, or exclusive-of-void. And examples of that are clear from what preceded. His statement: (He contradicted the universal negative with the particular affirmative) i.e., because their saying: 'And Allah has not sent down to any human being anything' [al-An'am: 91] is equivalent to their saying: 'No human being has a book sent down upon him,' and His saying: 'Who sent down the Book which Moses brought' [al-An'am: 91] is equivalent to his saying: 'Some human beings have a book sent down upon them,' and this is a true particular affirmative that invalidates their false universal negative. His statement: (All that he mentioned) i.e., from the definition of contradiction and the conditions; except that in the conditional, one considers what it accepts of conditions, such as unity in the two terms, time, place, and the like; like difference in quantity not quality, for it [the conditional] does not accept that. The summary: a conditional is contradicted by another conditional that differs from it in quantity and quality, and agrees with it in genus and species. So the contradictory of the universal affirmative implicative is a particular negative implicative, and the contradictory of the universal negative implicative is a particular affirmative implicative. And the coincidental follows the implicative in that. And the contradictory of the universal affirmative adversative is a particular negative adversative. And so on. His statement: (And examples of that are clear from what preceded) for example, our saying: 'Whenever this is a human, it is an animal,' its contradictory: 'It may not be that if it is a human, it is an animal.' And our saying: 'Absolutely not if this is a human, it is a stone,' its contradictory: 'It may be that if it is a human, it is a stone.' And the reverse in both. And the contradictory of the indefinite is the contradictory of the particular. And our saying: 'Always either the number is even or it is odd,' its contradictory: 'It may not be either the number is even or it is odd.' And our saying: 'Absolutely not either the thing is a human or it is rational,' its contradictory: 'It may be either ... etc.' And the indefinite is like the particular.
Source pages p. 648

Commentary
This passage is a commentary by al-Bannānī on al-Sullam al-Murawnaq, expanding the rules of contradiction (tanāquḍ) to conditional propositions. The author first states that all the conditions for contradiction in categorical propositions also apply to conditionals. However, an additional condition of 'unity' (ittiḥād) is required: in the genus, the two propositions must agree in being conjunctive or disjunctive; in the species, for conjunctives they must agree in being implicative (luzūmiyya) or coincidental (ittifāqiyya), and for disjunctives they must agree in being adversative (ʿinādiyya), coincidental, genuine (ḥaqīqiyya), exclusive-of-combination (māniʿat jamʿ), or exclusive-of-void (māniʿat khuluw). Then he explains a specific example from the Qur'an: the Jews' claim 'Allah has not sent down anything to any human' is a universal negative; Allah's question 'Who sent down the Book...' implies a true particular affirmative (some humans received a book), which contradicts the universal negative. This is a valid contradiction because the conditions are met. The commentary then summarizes: a conditional is contradicted by another that differs in quantity and quality but agrees in genus and species. For implicative conjunctives, the contradictory pairs are as in categoricals: A vs O, E vs I. Coincidentals follow the same pattern. For disjunctives, adversatives follow similar rules. Examples are given: 'Whenever it is a human, it is an animal' (A) is contradicted by 'It may not be that if it is a human, it is an animal' (O). Similarly for negative universals. Indefinite propositions are treated like particulars. This passage clarifies that contradiction in conditionals requires not only the standard conditions (unity of subject, predicate, time, place) but also agreement in the specific type of conditional relation. It guards against the misunderstanding that any two contrary conditionals automatically contradict each other regardless of their internal logical form.

Definitions and Types of Simple Conversion ('Aks Mustawī)
Bannani defines 'aks (conversion) linguistically and technically, lists its three types, and defines 'aks mustawī.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'aks (conversion) linguistically and technically, identify its three types, and explain the rule for converting universal affirmative propositions.

Core Idea
Simple conversion preserves truth by swapping subject and predicate, but universal affirmatives become particular affirmatives.

This passage from al-Bannānī's commentary on al-Sullam al-Murawnaq lays the foundation for one of the key operations in propositional logic: immediate inference through conversion. Understanding 'aks is essential for evaluating arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence. Let us walk through the definitions and types so you can apply them to real Islamic examples.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish linguistic vs. technical definition
Notice that 'aks linguistically means turning a thing over, but technically it is ambiguous: it can refer to the act of converting or the converted proposition itself. This distinction matters for how we use the term in logical analysis.
2
Identify the three types of conversion
The passage lists three types: simple conversion (عكس مستو), conversion by contradictory of same quality (عكس نقيض موافق), and conversion by contradictory of different quality (عكس نقيض مخالف). Only the first is covered because it is most common.
3
Understand the definition of simple conversion
Simple conversion is defined as swapping the subject and predicate of a proposition while preserving truth, quality, and quantity, except for universal affirmatives, which become particular affirmatives. The conversion must be necessary: if the original is true, the convert is true.
The condition 'preserving truth' means that the converted proposition follows necessarily from the original.
4
Note the exception for universal affirmatives
For universal affirmative (A) propositions like 'Every intoxicant is forbidden', the simple conversion is not 'Every forbidden thing is intoxicant' (which would be false), but rather 'Some forbidden things are intoxicants' (particular affirmative, I). This is because otherwise truth would not be preserved.
5
Recognize the rationale for naming
The three types are named based on whether the converted terms are the same as the original or their contradictories, and whether the quality (affirmative/negative) is preserved or changed. 'Simple' conversion uses the same terms; 'conversion by contradictory' replaces terms with their contradictories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في العكس المستوي هذا هو البحث الثاني من أحكام القضايا . والعكس في اللغة: قلب الشيء؛ بجعل أوله آخره، أو أعلاه أسفله، ونحو ذلك»؛ مصدر: «عكسه ، يعكسه» من باب : ضرب». وفي الاصطلاح هو مشترك بين المعنى المصدر واسم المفعول، وكل منهما ينقسم إلى ثلاثة أقسام : عكس مستو، وعكس نقيض موافق، وعكس نقيض مخالف، واقتصر المصنف على الأول وهو المستوي ؛ لكثرة استعماله دون غيره. وأشار إلى تعريفه بالمعنى المصدر بقوله [46/1] : (العكس : قلب جزأي القضية مع بقاء الصدق والكيفية والكم؛ إلا الموجب الكلية فعوضوا الموجب الجزئية ومراده بـ «قلب جزأي القضية : تبديل كل واحد من طرفي القضية ذات الترتيب الطبيعي بعين الآخر، مع بقاء الصدق والكيف على وجه اللزوم . قوله : (قلب الشيء) أي: مطلق القلب؛ أي: التحويل. قوله : (وفي الاصطلاح هو مشترك أي : وأما في اللغة فلا اشتراك؛ إذ هو حقيقة في المصدر، فإن أطلق على المعكوس إليه فمجاز مرسل، ثم صار في الاصطلاح حقيقة عرفية. قوله : (عكس مستو ... إلخ) سمي : «مستويا»؛ لاستواء الأصل والعكس في ذات الطرفين، وإن اختلف الترتيب. وسمي الثاني : عكس نقيض؛ لوقوع التبديل فيه بنقيض الطرفين، و: «موافقاً»؛ لموافقته العكس للأصل في الكيف. الثالث : «عكس نقيض؛ لكون التبديل في أحد الجزأين بالنقيض، و: «مخالفاً»؛ وسمي لمخالفة العكس الأصل في الكيف. قوله : (ذات الترتيب الطبيعي) أي : المعنوي .
Chapter on Simple Conversion. This is the second topic from the rulings of propositions. 'Aks in language: to turn a thing over; by making its first its last, or its top its bottom, and the like; a verbal noun from ['akasahu, ya'kusuhu] of the pattern [ḍaraba]. In technical terminology it is ambiguous between the verbal noun and the passive participle. And each of them is divided into three divisions: simple conversion, conversion by contradictory of the same quality, and conversion by contradictory of different quality. The author limited himself to the first, which is simple, because of its frequent use over others. He alluded to its definition in the verbal sense by his saying [46/1]: ('Aks is the turning over of the two parts of a proposition while preserving truth, quality, and quantity; except for the universal affirmative, for they substitute the particular affirmative.) And his intention by 'turning over of the two parts of a proposition' is: to exchange each of the two terms of the proposition having natural order for the very other, while preserving truth and quality in a necessary manner. His statement: (turning over of a thing) i.e., absolute turning; i.e., changing. His statement: (In technical terminology it is ambiguous) i.e., whereas in language it is not ambiguous; for it is a literal truth in the verbal noun, and if applied to the thing converted to, then it is a transferred metaphor; then in technical terminology it became a conventional truth. His statement: (Simple conversion ... etc.) It is called 'simple' because the original and the converted are equal in the essence of the two terms, though the order differs. And the second is called 'conversion by contradictory' because the substitution occurs with the contradictory of the two terms, and 'of the same quality' because the conversion agrees with the original in quality. The third is called 'conversion by contradictory' because the substitution is in one of the two parts with the contradictory, and 'of different quality' because the conversion differs from the original in quality. His statement: (having natural order) i.e., mental order.
Source pages p. 649

Commentary
This passage is a commentary by al-Bannānī on the chapter on simple conversion (al-ʿaks al-mustawī) from al-Sullam al-Murawnaq. It begins by stating that this is the second topic of the rulings of propositions. The definition of 'aks is given: linguistically, it means turning a thing over, like making its first last or its top bottom. Technically, the term is ambiguous — it can refer to the verbal noun (the act of converting) or the passive participle (the converted proposition). Each of these senses is divided into three types: simple conversion (ʿaks mustawī), conversion by contradictory of the same quality (ʿaks naqīḍ muwāfiq), and conversion by contradictory of different quality (ʿaks naqīḍ mukhālif). The author (al-Sullam's author) only discusses simple conversion because it is most frequently used. Then the matn line is quoted: 'al-ʿaks: qalbu juz'ay al-qaḍiyya...' which defines conversion as swapping the subject and predicate while preserving truth (ṣidq), quality (kayfiyya), and quantity (kamm), except that for universal affirmative, they substitute a particular affirmative. Bannānī explains that 'turning over the two parts' means exchanging each term for the other, given the natural order (the subject before predicate). This must be done necessarily (ʿalā wajh al-luzūm) — i.e., if the original is true, the converted must be true. The commentary then clarifies the linguistic and technical usage: in language, 'aks is only literal for the verbal noun; applying it to the converted proposition is a transferred sense. Then it explains the naming of the three types: simple conversion is so called because the original and the converted share the very same terms, though in reverse order; the second type is called 'conversion by contradictory of same quality' because the terms are replaced by their contradictories but the quality (affirmative/negative) remains the same; the third type is called 'conversion by contradictory of different quality' because one term is replaced by its contradictory and the quality changes. Finally, 'natural order' refers to the mental order (al-maʿnawī), i.e., the subject naturally precedes the predicate in our understanding. This passage provides the foundational definitions for the study of immediate inference through conversion.

قلب
الصدق

Key Takeaway
Simple conversion (al-ʿaks al-mustawī) is an immediate inference that swaps subject and predicate while preserving truth, with the important exception that universal affirmatives become particular affirmatives.

Immediate Inference

Definition of Simple Conversion and the Exception for Universal Affirmatives
Definition of simple conversion with exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define simple conversion (al-'aks al-mustawi) and correctly apply the exception rule: universal affirmative converts to particular affirmative.

Core Idea
Simple conversion swaps subject and predicate, but universal affirmatives become particular affirmatives to preserve truth.

In this passage, the author defines the logical operation of simple conversion, which is the most common type of immediate inference. He gives the precise rule: you may swap the subject and predicate of a proposition, keeping its quality and quantity, except that a universal affirmative must become a particular affirmative to preserve truth. Let's unpack this definition step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition's parts
Every categorical proposition has a subject and predicate (e.g., 'Every intoxicant is haram' – subject: intoxicant, predicate: haram).
2
Swap the two parts
Reverse their order to form a new proposition (e.g., 'Every haram is intoxicant' is the swapped form).
3
Check truth preservation
For universal affirmative, the swapped universal 'Every haram is intoxicant' is false because not all haram is intoxicant. So we must convert it to particular affirmative 'Some haram is intoxicant' which is true.
4
Apply the rule to other proposition types
For universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative, the swapped proposition retains the same quantity and quality and truth. For example, 'No alcoholic drink is permissible' converts to 'No permissible thing is an alcoholic drink'.
The exception only applies to universal affirmative.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في العكس المستوي
هذا هو البحث الثاني من أحكام القضايا. والعكس في اللغة: قلب الشيء؛ بجعل أوله آخره، أو أعلاه أسفله، ونحو ذلك؛ مصدر: «عكسه، يعكسه» من باب: ضرب.
وفي الاصطلاح هو مشترك بين المعنى المصدري واسم المفعول، وكل منهما ينقسم إلى ثلاثة أقسام: عكس مستو، وعكس نقيض موافق، وعكس نقيض مخالف، واقتصر المصنف على الأول وهو المستوي؛ لكثرة استعماله دون غيره.
وأشار إلى تعريفه بالمعنى المصدر بقوله: (العكس: قلب جزأي القضية مع بقاء الصدق والكيفية والكم؛ إلا الموجبة الكلية فعودوها الموجب الجزئية). ومراده بـ«قلب جزأي القضية»: تبديل كل واحد من طرفي القضية ذات الترتيب الطبيعي بعين الآخر، مع بقاء الصدق والكيف على وجه اللزوم.
ثم شرح: (قوله: (قلب الشيء) أي: مطلق القلب؛ أي: التحويل).
(قوله: (وفي الاصطلاح هو مشترك) أي: وأما في اللغة فلا اشتراك؛ إذ هو حقيقة في المصدر، فإن أطلق على المعكوس إليه فمجاز مرسل، ثم صار في الاصطلاح حقيقة عرفية).
(قوله: (عكس مستو... إلخ) سمي: «مستويا»؛ لاستواء الأصل والعكس في ذات الطرفين، وإن اختلف الترتيب. وسمي الثاني: عكس نقيض؛ لوقوع التبديل فيه بنقيض الطرفين، و: «موافقاً»؛ لموافقته العكس للأصل في الكيف. الثالث: «عكس نقيض؛ لكون التبديل في أحد الجزأين بالنقيض، و: «مخالفاً»؛ وسمي لمخالفة العكس الأصل في الكيف).
(قوله: (ذات الترتيب الطبيعي) أي: المعنوي).
فخرج بـ طرفي القضية: تبديل السور مثلاً والجهة، وتبديل أحد الطرفين فقط؛ كقولك: «زيد إنسان، زيد عالم».
ودخل في القضية الحملية والمتصلة، وأما المنفصلة فلا عكس لها، كما سينبه عليه بقوله: والعكس في مرتب بالطبع... البيت، وهو محترز التقييد بذات الترتيب الطبيعي.
وقولنا: بعين الآخر مخرج لعكس النقيض بنوعيه؛ لأن التبديل فيهما كما يأتي ليس بعين كل من الطرفين.
معنى الترتيب في ذلك:
أن الحملية: يقتضي الطبع تقديم موضوعها وتأخير محمولها؛ إذ الثاني مسوق للأول، وتعقل المسوق ثان عن تعقل المسوق له.
والمتصلة أيضاً: يقتضي الطبع تقديم مقدمها وتأخير تاليها؛ لأن الأول طالب للصحبة وملزوم، والثاني مطلوب لها ولازم، وتعقل الملزوم والطالب سابق على تعقل اللازم والمطلوب. بخلاف المنفصلة: فترتيب طرفيها لفظي فقط، فلك أن تقدم وتؤخر ما شئت منهما، والمعنى بحاله لا يتبدل؛ وهذا الذي ذكره وهو مرتضى المحققين، وزعم القطب أنها تنعكس؛ لأن الحكم في نحو: «إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً، وإما أن يكون فرداً بمعاندة الزوجية للفردية، وفي عكسه بمعاندة الفردية للزوجية، وسيأتي للشارح رده.
ثم إن هذا القيد كما يخرج المنفصلة كما ذكر، يخرج أيضاً الاتفاقية المتصلة؛ لأن ترتيب طرفيها لفظي، لا معنوي؛ نبه على ذلك السعد.
قوله: (وقولنا: بعين الآخر مخرج لعكس النقيض... إلخ) لا يحتاج لهذا القيد؛ لأن عكس النقيض بنوعيه خرج بإضافة «قلب» لـ «جزأي القضية» في كلام الناظم لعدم بقاء عين الطرفين فيهما؛ لأن قوله: قلب جزأي القضية مخرج لقلب غير جزأيها، وحينئذ فلا يحتاج إلى تقييد القلب بالعين كما فعل الشارح. وموجب ذلك له: توهم اتحاد التعبير بالتبديل، كما فعل صاحب «المختصر»، والتعبير بالقلب كما فعل الناظم، والفرق بينهما كما قال شيخنا سيدي الطيب: أبين من أن يبين.
Chapter on Simple Conversion. This is the second topic of the rulings of propositions. Linguistically, 'aks (conversion) is the overturning of a thing by making its first its last, or its top its bottom, and similar; it is the masdar of 'akasahu, ya'kusuhu from the paradigm of daraba. Technically, it is shared between the masdar meaning and the passive participle meaning, and each of them divides into three divisions: simple conversion, conversion of the congruent opposite, and conversion of the incongruent opposite. The author confined himself to the first, i.e., simple conversion, due to its frequent usage over others. He pointed to its definition in the masdar sense by his saying: (Conversion is the overturning of the two parts of a proposition while preserving truth, quality, and quantity, except for the universal affirmative, for they turn it into the particular affirmative.) And his intent by 'overturning the two parts of the proposition' is the substitution of each one of the two extremes of the proposition possessing natural order with the very other, while preserving truth and quality in a necessary manner. Then the commentary: His statement: (Overturning of a thing) means absolute overturning, i.e., transformation. His statement: (Technically it is shared) means: as for language, there is no sharing; for it is literal in the masdar, and if it is applied to the converted-to, it is a transferred metaphor, then it became teknis a customary literal meaning. His statement: (Simple conversion, etc.) It is called 'simple' because of the equality of the original and the conversion in the identity of the two extremes, even if the order differs. The second is called 'conversion of the opposite' because of the substitution in it by the opposite of the two extremes, and 'congruent' due to its congruence with the original in quality. The third: 'conversion of the opposite' because the substitution is in one of the two parts by the opposite, and 'incongruent' due to its opposition to the original in quality. His statement: (possessing natural order) means meaningful order. So by 'the two extremes of the proposition' is excluded: the substitution of the quantifier, for example, and the modality, and the substitution of only one of the two extremes, like your saying: 'Zayd is human, Zayd is knowledgeable.' Included are categorical and conditional propositions; as for disjunctive, it has no conversion, as he will alert to by his saying: 'And conversion is in that which is naturally ordered...' the verse, and it is a guard of the restriction by 'possessing natural order'. And our saying: 'with the very other' excludes conversion of the opposite of both types because the substitution in them, as will come, is not with the very each of the two extremes. The meaning of order therein: That the categorical requires by nature the priority of its subject and the posteriority of its predicate; for the second is led to the first, and the understanding of the led comes second after the understanding of the leader. And the conditional also requires by nature the priority of its antecedent and the posteriority of its consequent; because the first is a seeker of companionship and a necessary condition, and the second is sought for it and a necessary consequence, and the understanding of the necessary condition and the seeker precedes the understanding of the necessary consequence and the sought. Unlike the disjunctive: for the order of its two sides is merely verbal, so you may give priority and posteriority as you wish to either of them, and the meaning remains unchanged; this is what he mentioned and it is the approved view of the scholars. Al-Qutb claimed that it converts because the judgment in, for example, 'Either the number is even or it is odd,' is due to the opposition of evenness to oddness, and in its converse due to the opposition of oddness to evenness, and the commentator's refutation will come. Then, this restriction, as it excludes the disjunctive as mentioned, also excludes the accidental conditional; for the order of its two sides is verbal, not meaningful; al-Sa'd alerted to that. His statement: (And our saying: 'with the very other' excludes conversion of the opposite... etc.) This restriction is not needed because conversion of the opposite of both types is excluded by the addition of 'overturning' to 'the two parts of the proposition' in the author's speech due to the non-preservation of the identicalness of the two extremes therein; because his saying 'overturning of the two parts of the proposition' excludes overturning of other than its two parts, and then there is no need to restrict the overturning with 'very' as the commentator did. The reason for his doing so is the illusion of unity of expression with 'substitution' as the author of al-Mukhtasar did, and the expression with 'overturning' as the author did, and the difference between them, as our master al-Tayyib said, is clearer than can be clarified.
Source pages p. 649

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory exposition of 'simple conversion' (al-'aks al-mustawi) from a commentary (sharh) on the matn al-Sullam al-Munawraq. The author first gives the linguistic meaning of 'aks as overturning, then explains that technically it is ambiguous between the verbal noun (masdar) and the passive participle (ism al-maf'ul). Each of these two meanings divides into three types: simple conversion, conversion of the congruent opposite, and conversion of the incongruent opposite. The author of the matn focuses only on simple conversion due to its frequent use. The definition given by the matn is: 'Conversion is the overturning of the two parts of a proposition while preserving truth, quality, and quantity, except that the universal affirmative is turned into the particular affirmative.' The commentator clarifies that 'overturning the two parts' means swapping the subject and predicate (the extremes) of a proposition that has a natural order, such that each extreme is replaced by the very other (not a contradictory or different term), while necessarily preserving truth and quality. The exception for universal affirmative (e.g., 'Every A is B' converts to 'Some B is A') is because if we converted it to a universal affirmative—'Every B is A'—it may be false; e.g., 'Every intoxicant is haram' does not imply 'Every haram is intoxicant', so we turn it to particular affirmative 'Some haram is intoxicant' which is true. The commentator then explains the terminology: 'simple' (mustawi) because the original and converted share the same extremes, only order changes; 'conversion of the opposite' (naqid) because the conversion uses the contradictory of each extreme; 'congruent' (muwafiq) because quality is preserved; 'incongruent' (mukhalif) because quality changes. The condition of 'natural order' means that the subject is naturally prior and the predicate posterior. This excludes propositions where the order is merely verbal, such as disjunctive conditionals. The commentator notes that categorical (hamli) and conditional conjunctive (muttasila) propositions have natural order; disjunctive (munfasila) does not, so it has no conversion. Also, 'accidental conditional' (ittifaqiyya muttasila) is excluded because its order is only verbal. The commentator further refutes al-Qutb's claim that disjunctives convert, using the example of 'either even or odd', but the accepted view is that conversion does not apply. Finally, the commentator discusses that the phrase 'with the very other' (bi'aynil akhar) is redundant because the matn's expression 'overturning the two parts' already implies using the same extremes, not their opposites. The difference between 'substitution' (tabdeel) and 'overturning' (qalb) is mentioned as obvious.

العكس المستوي
العكس
القلب

Checkpoint
According to the definition of simple conversion, why must a universal affirmative (e.g., 'Every intoxicant is haram') be converted to a particular affirmative ('Some haram is intoxicant')?
A) Because the original proposition is false.
B) Because the direct converted universal ('Every haram is intoxicant') would be false, violating truth preservation.
C) Because the quality must change.
D) Because the quantity must remain the same.

Key Takeaway
Simple conversion swaps subject and predicate, but universal affirmative becomes particular affirmative to preserve truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Convert the following categorical propositions using simple conversion, applying the exception rule where necessary: (a) 'Every alcoholic drink is impure (najis).' (b) 'No gambling is permissible.' (c) 'Some animals are halal.' (d) 'Some believers are not scholars.'

The Three Types of Conversion: Simple, Congruent Opposite, Incongruent Opposite
Enumeration of three types of conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of conversion (al-'aks) and identify which type is most commonly used.

Core Idea
Conversion has three types: simple (swap same terms), congruent opposite (swap contradictories preserving quality), and incongruent opposite (swap one contradictory changing quality).

The author mentions that 'aks technically divides into three types. He focuses only on the first – simple conversion – because it is most frequent. But it is important to understand the other two as they appear in some logical discussions. Let's examine each type as explained in the passage.

Guided Path
1
Understand the basis of division
Conversion can be done by swapping the two extremes with the very same terms (simple), or by swapping each extreme with its contradictory (naqid). The latter divides into two based on whether the quality (affirmation/negation) is preserved or changed.
2
Simple conversion ('aks mustawi)
Swap subject and predicate exactly. Preserves quality and quantity, except universal affirmative becomes particular. Example: 'Every A is B' → 'Some B is A'.
3
Congruent opposite conversion ('aks naqid muwafiq)
Swap each term with its contradictory (e.g., A with non-A, B with non-B), but keep the quality the same. So 'Every A is B' becomes 'Every non-B is non-A' (by contraposition). This is often called contraposition in modern logic.
4
Incongruent opposite conversion ('aks naqid mukhalif)
Swap only one term with its contradictory, and change the quality. For example, 'Every A is B' might become 'No non-A is B' or something. The passage doesn't give a specific example, but it is defined.
5
Identify usage
The author says the simple type is most commonly used; the other two are rarely needed in standard argumentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في العكس المستوي
هذا هو البحث الثاني من أحكام القضايا. والعكس في اللغة: قلب الشيء؛ بجعل أوله آخره، أو أعلاه أسفله، ونحو ذلك؛ مصدر: «عكسه، يعكسه» من باب: ضرب.
وفي الاصطلاح هو مشترك بين المعنى المصدري واسم المفعول، وكل منهما ينقسم إلى ثلاثة أقسام: عكس مستو، وعكس نقيض موافق، وعكس نقيض مخالف، واقتصر المصنف على الأول وهو المستوي؛ لكثرة استعماله دون غيره.
وأشار إلى تعريفه بالمعنى المصدر بقوله: (العكس: قلب جزأي القضية مع بقاء الصدق والكيفية والكم؛ إلا الموجبة الكلية فعودوها الموجب الجزئية). ومراده بـ«قلب جزأي القضية»: تبديل كل واحد من طرفي القضية ذات الترتيب الطبيعي بعين الآخر، مع بقاء الصدق والكيف على وجه اللزوم.
ثم شرح: (قوله: (قلب الشيء) أي: مطلق القلب؛ أي: التحويل).
(قوله: (وفي الاصطلاح هو مشترك) أي: وأما في اللغة فلا اشتراك؛ إذ هو حقيقة في المصدر، فإن أطلق على المعكوس إليه فمجاز مرسل، ثم صار في الاصطلاح حقيقة عرفية).
(قوله: (عكس مستو... إلخ) سمي: «مستويا»؛ لاستواء الأصل والعكس في ذات الطرفين، وإن اختلف الترتيب. وسمي الثاني: عكس نقيض؛ لوقوع التبديل فيه بنقيض الطرفين، و: «موافقاً»؛ لموافقته العكس للأصل في الكيف. الثالث: «عكس نقيض؛ لكون التبديل في أحد الجزأين بالنقيض، و: «مخالفاً»؛ وسمي لمخالفة العكس الأصل في الكيف).
(قوله: (ذات الترتيب الطبيعي) أي: المعنوي).
فخرج بـ طرفي القضية: تبديل السور مثلاً والجهة، وتبديل أحد الطرفين فقط؛ كقولك: «زيد إنسان، زيد عالم».
ودخل في القضية الحملية والمتصلة، وأما المنفصلة فلا عكس لها، كما سينبه عليه بقوله: والعكس في مرتب بالطبع... البيت، وهو محترز التقييد بذات الترتيب الطبيعي.
وقولنا: بعين الآخر مخرج لعكس النقيض بنوعيه؛ لأن التبديل فيهما كما يأتي ليس بعين كل من الطرفين.
معنى الترتيب في ذلك:
أن الحملية: يقتضي الطبع تقديم موضوعها وتأخير محمولها؛ إذ الثاني مسوق للأول، وتعقل المسوق ثان عن تعقل المسوق له.
والمتصلة أيضاً: يقتضي الطبع تقديم مقدمها وتأخير تاليها؛ لأن الأول طالب للصحبة وملزوم، والثاني مطلوب لها ولازم، وتعقل الملزوم والطالب سابق على تعقل اللازم والمطلوب. بخلاف المنفصلة: فترتيب طرفيها لفظي فقط، فلك أن تقدم وتؤخر ما شئت منهما، والمعنى بحاله لا يتبدل؛ وهذا الذي ذكره وهو مرتضى المحققين، وزعم القطب أنها تنعكس؛ لأن الحكم في نحو: «إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً، وإما أن يكون فرداً بمعاندة الزوجية للفردية، وفي عكسه بمعاندة الفردية للزوجية، وسيأتي للشارح رده.
ثم إن هذا القيد كما يخرج المنفصلة كما ذكر، يخرج أيضاً الاتفاقية المتصلة؛ لأن ترتيب طرفيها لفظي، لا معنوي؛ نبه على ذلك السعد.
قوله: (وقولنا: بعين الآخر مخرج لعكس النقيض... إلخ) لا يحتاج لهذا القيد؛ لأن عكس النقيض بنوعيه خرج بإضافة «قلب» لـ «جزأي القضية» في كلام الناظم لعدم بقاء عين الطرفين فيهما؛ لأن قوله: قلب جزأي القضية مخرج لقلب غير جزأيها، وحينئذ فلا يحتاج إلى تقييد القلب بالعين كما فعل الشارح. وموجب ذلك له: توهم اتحاد التعبير بالتبديل، كما فعل صاحب «المختصر»، والتعبير بالقلب كما فعل الناظم، والفرق بينهما كما قال شيخنا سيدي الطيب: أبين من أن يبين.
Chapter on Simple Conversion. This is the second topic of the rulings of propositions. Linguistically, 'aks (conversion) is the overturning of a thing by making its first its last, or its top its bottom, and similar; it is the masdar of 'akasahu, ya'kusuhu from the paradigm of daraba. Technically, it is shared between the masdar meaning and the passive participle meaning, and each of them divides into three divisions: simple conversion, conversion of the congruent opposite, and conversion of the incongruent opposite. The author confined himself to the first, i.e., simple conversion, due to its frequent usage over others. He pointed to its definition in the masdar sense by his saying: (Conversion is the overturning of the two parts of a proposition while preserving truth, quality, and quantity, except for the universal affirmative, for they turn it into the particular affirmative.) And his intent by 'overturning the two parts of the proposition' is the substitution of each one of the two extremes of the proposition possessing natural order with the very other, while preserving truth and quality in a necessary manner. Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory exposition of 'simple conversion' (al-'aks al-mustawi) from a commentary (sharh) on the matn al-Sullam al-Munawraq. ?

For congruent opposite (naqid muwafiq): 'Every insincere (non-sincere) is a non-believer'. For incongruent opposite (naqid mukhalif): (choose one) e.g., 'No non-believer is sincere' (if we swap only one contradictory and change quality).

Natural Order and Why Disjunctive Propositions Have No Conversion
Condition of natural order and exclusion of disjunctives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why natural order (al-tartib al-tabi'i) is necessary for conversion and why disjunctive conditionals (munfasila) and accidental conditionals do not convert.

Core Idea
Only propositions with natural meaningful order – where one part is inherently prior – can be converted; disjunctives lack this order, so they have no conversion.

A key condition for conversion mentioned in the passage is that the proposition must have 'natural order' (al-tartib al-tabi'i). This means the subject/antecedent is naturally prior and the predicate/consequent is posterior. For categorical and conjunctive conditionals, this holds. But for disjunctive conditionals, the order is merely verbal, so conversion is not possible. The commentator also discusses a debate about this.

Guided Path
1
Identify natural order
For categoricals, the subject is conceptually prior to the predicate (e.g., 'Zayd' is understood before 'standing'). For conditionals, the antecedent is prior to the consequent.
2
Understand conversion requirement
Conversion swaps the parts, but for the conversion to preserve meaning and truth, the original order must be meaningful; otherwise, swapping gives a different meaning.
3
See why disjunctives fail
In a disjunctive like 'Either the number is even or odd', the order doesn't matter; you can say 'Either odd or even' with the same meaning. So conversion would not change anything and is not a logical operation. The commentator notes that al-Qutb claimed they do convert, but the majority rejects that.
4
Accidental conditionals
Also excluded because the connection between antecedent and consequent is not necessary; the order is just verbal.

Source Text

معنى الترتيب في ذلك:
أن الحملية: يقتضي الطبع تقديم موضوعها وتأخير محمولها؛ إذ الثاني مسوق للأول، وتعقل المسوق ثان عن تعقل المسوق له.
والمتصلة أيضاً: يقتضي الطبع تقديم مقدمها وتأخير تاليها؛ لأن الأول طالب للصحبة وملزوم، والثاني مطلوب لها ولازم، وتعقل الملزوم والطالب سابق على تعقل اللازم والمطلوب. بخلاف المنفصلة: فترتيب طرفيها لفظي فقط، فلك أن تقدم وتؤخر ما شئت منهما، والمعنى بحاله لا يتبدل؛ وهذا الذي ذكره وهو مرتضى المحققين، وزعم القطب أنها تنعكس؛ لأن الحكم في نحو: «إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً، وإما أن يكون فرداً بمعاندة الزوجية للفردية، وفي عكسه بمعاندة الفردية للزوجية، وسيأتي للشارح رده.
ثم إن هذا القيد كما يخرج المنفصلة كما ذكر، يخرج أيضاً الاتفاقية المتصلة؛ لأن ترتيب طرفيها لفظي، لا معنوي؛ نبه على ذلك السعد.
قوله: (وقولنا: بعين الآخر مخرج لعكس النقيض... إلخ) لا يحتاج لهذا القيد؛ لأن عكس النقيض بنوعيه خرج بإضافة «قلب» لـ «جزأي القضية» في كلام الناظم لعدم بقاء عين الطرفين فيهما؛ لأن قوله: قلب جزأي القضية مخرج لقلب غير جزأيها، وحينئذ فلا يحتاج إلى تقييد القلب بالعين كما فعل الشارح. وموجب ذلك له: توهم اتحاد التعبير بالتبديل، كما فعل صاحب «المختصر»، والتعبير بالقلب كما فعل الناظم، والفرق بينهما كما قال شيخنا سيدي الطيب: أبين من أن يبين.
Chapter on Simple Conversion. This is the second topic of the rulings of propositions. Linguistically, 'aks (conversion) is the overturning of a thing by making its first its last, or its top its bottom, and similar; it is the masdar of 'akasahu, ya'kusuhu from the paradigm of daraba. Technically, it is shared between the masdar meaning and the passive participle meaning, and each of them divides into three divisions: simple conversion, conversion of the congruent opposite, and conversion of the incongruent opposite. The author confined himself to the first, i.e., simple conversion, due to its frequent usage over others. He pointed to its definition in the masdar sense by his saying: (Conversion is the overturning of the two parts of a proposition while preserving truth, quality, and quantity, except for the universal affirmative, for they turn it into the particular affirmative.) And his intent by 'overturning the two parts of the proposition' is the substitution of each one of the two extremes of the proposition possessing natural order with the very other, while preserving truth and quality in a necessary manner. Then the commentary: His statement: (Overturning of a thing) means absolute overturning, i.e., transformation. His statement: (Technically it is shared) means: as for language, there is no sharing; for it is literal in the masdar, and if it is applied to the converted-to, it is a transferred metaphor, then it became teknis a customary literal meaning. His statement: (Simple conversion, etc.) It is called 'simple' because of the equality of the original and the conversion in the identity of the two extremes, even if the order differs. The second is called 'conversion of the opposite' because of the substitution in it by the opposite of the two extremes, and 'congruent' due to its congruence with the original in quality. The third: 'conversion of the opposite' because the substitution is in one of the two parts by the opposite, and 'incongruent' due to its opposition to the original in quality. His statement: (possessing natural order) means meaningful order. So by 'the two extremes of the proposition' is excluded: the substitution of the quantifier, for example, and the modality, and the substitution of only one of the two extremes, like your saying: 'Zayd is human, Zayd is knowledgeable.' Included are categorical and conditional propositions; as for disjunctive, it has no conversion, as he will alert to by his saying: 'And conversion is in that which is naturally ordered...' the verse, and it is a guard of the restriction by 'possessing natural order'. And our saying: 'with the very other' excludes conversion of the opposite of both types because the substitution in them, as will come, is not with the very each of the two extremes. The meaning of order therein: That the categorical requires by nature the priority of its subject and the posteriority of its predicate; for the second is led to the first, and the understanding of the led comes second after the understanding of the leader. And the conditional also requires by nature the priority of its antecedent and the posteriority of its consequent; because the first is a seeker of companionship and a necessary condition, and the second is sought for it and a necessary consequence, and the understanding of the necessary condition and the seeker precedes the understanding of the necessary consequence and the sought. Unlike the disjunctive: for the order of its two sides is merely verbal, so you may give priority and posteriority as you wish to either of them, and the meaning remains unchanged; this is what he mentioned and it is the approved view of the scholars. Al-Qutb claimed that it converts because the judgment in, for example, 'Either the number is even or it is odd,' is due to the opposition of evenness to oddness, and in its converse due to the opposition of oddness to evenness, and the commentator's refutation will come. Then, this restriction, as it excludes the disjunctive as mentioned, also excludes the accidental conditional; for the order of its two sides is verbal, not meaningful; al-Sa'd alerted to that. His statement: (And our saying: 'with the very other' excludes conversion of the opposite... etc.) This restriction is not needed because conversion of the opposite of both types is excluded by the addition of 'overturning' to 'the two parts of the proposition' in the author's speech due to the non-preservation of the identicalness of the two extremes therein; because his saying 'overturning of the two parts of the proposition' excludes overturning of other than its two parts, and then there is no need to restrict the overturning with 'very' as the commentator did. The reason for his doing so is the illusion of unity of expression with 'substitution' as the author of al-Mukhtasar did, and the expression with 'overturning' as the author did, and the difference between them, as our master al-Tayyib said, is clearer than can be clarified.
Source pages p. 649

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory exposition of 'simple conversion' (al-'aks al-mustawi) from a commentary (sharh) on the matn al-Sullam al-Munawraq. The author first gives the linguistic meaning of 'aks as overturning, then explains that technically it is ambiguous between the verbal noun (masdar) and the passive participle (ism al-maf'ul). Each of these two meanings divides into three types: simple conversion, conversion of the congruent opposite, and conversion of the incongruent opposite. The author of the matn focuses only on simple conversion due to its frequent use. The definition given by the matn is: 'Conversion is the overturning of the two parts of a proposition while preserving truth, quality, and quantity, except that the universal affirmative is turned into the particular affirmative.' The commentator clarifies that 'overturning the two parts' means swapping the subject and predicate (the extremes) of a proposition that has a natural order, such that each extreme is replaced by the very other (not a contradictory or different term), while necessarily preserving truth and quality. The exception for universal affirmative (e.g., 'Every A is B' converts to 'Some B is A') is because if we converted it to a universal affirmative—'Every B is A'—it may be false; e.g., 'Every intoxicant is haram' does not imply 'Every haram is intoxicant', so we turn it to particular affirmative 'Some haram is intoxicant' which is true. The commentator then explains the terminology: 'simple' (mustawi) because the original and converted share the same extremes, only order changes; 'conversion of the opposite' (naqid) because the conversion uses the contradictory of each extreme; 'congruent' (muwafiq) because quality is preserved; 'incongruent' (mukhalif) because quality changes. The condition of 'natural order' means that the subject is naturally prior and the predicate posterior. This excludes propositions where the order is merely verbal, such as disjunctive conditionals. The commentator notes that categorical (hamli) and conditional conjunctive (muttasila) propositions have natural order; disjunctive (munfasila) does not, so it has no conversion. Also, 'accidental conditional' (ittifaqiyya muttasila) is excluded because its order is only verbal. The commentator further refutes al-Qutb's claim that disjunctives convert, using the example of 'either even or odd', but the accepted view is that conversion does not apply. Finally, the commentator discusses that the phrase 'with the very other' (bi'aynil akhar) is redundant because the matn's expression 'overturning the two parts' already implies using the same extremes, not their opposites. The difference between 'substitution' (tabdeel) and 'overturning' (qalb) is mentioned as obvious.

الترتيب الطبيعي
الحملية

Key Takeaway
Only propositions with natural order – where the parts have a meaningful priority – can be legitimately converted.

Determine whether the following propositions can undergo simple conversion: (a) 'Every Muslim is a believer.' (b) 'If it rains, then the ground is wet.' (c) 'Either the prayer is valid or invalid.' (d) 'Some scholars are wise.' (Note: (c) is a disjunctive and cannot be converted; (b) is a conditional and can be converted by more complex rules; (d) is particular affirmative and converts to particular affirmative.)

Immediate Inference

Definition and Conditions of al-‘Aks (Conversion)
Author defines 'aks and explains conditions: natural order, exclusion of disjunctives and contraposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definition of al-‘aks (conversion) and list the conditions under which a proposition can be converted, including the requirement of natural order and the exclusion of disjunctives and contraposition.

Core Idea
Conversion requires natural order: only categorical and conditional propositions can be converted, not disjunctives.

In this passage, the commentator refines the classical definition of al-‘aks (conversion). He explains that conversion involves swapping the two parts of a proposition while preserving truth, but only if the proposition has a natural order between its parts. This excludes disjunctive propositions and also conversion by contraposition. Understanding these conditions is crucial for correctly applying immediate inference in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
1. Extract the core definition of al-‘aks
Find the phrase 'الْعَكْسُ: قَلْبُ جُزْأَيِ الْقَضِيَّةِ مَعَ بَقَاءِ الصِّدْقِ' (Conversion is turning over the two parts of the proposition while preserving truth). This is the basic definition.
The definition uses قَلْب (turning over) not تَبْدِيل (substitution), which is important.
2
2. Identify what the definition excludes
The definition excludes changing only the quantifier or modality, or changing only one part (e.g., 'Zayd is a human, Zayd is knowledgeable' where subject is not swapped). It also excludes conversion by contraposition, where the parts are not preserved identically.
The commentator argues that the phrasing 'turn over the two parts' already excludes contraposition, so adding 'with the identical other' is redundant.
3
3. Understand the condition of natural order
The passage explains that conversion applies only to propositions with natural order (الترتيب الطبيعي). In categoricals, subject precedes predicate by nature; in conditionals, antecedent precedes consequent. In disjunctives, the order is merely verbal, so they lack natural order and cannot be converted.
Accidental conditionals (where connection is extrinsic) also lack natural order and are excluded.
4
4. Distinguish simple conversion from contraposition
Simple conversion (al-‘aks al-mustawī) preserves the identical parts. Conversion by contraposition (عكس النقيض) swaps and negates the parts, so it is not considered simple conversion. The passage clarifies that the definition's wording already excludes contraposition.
The debate with al-Qutb about disjunctive conversion is mentioned; the commentator dismisses it because disjunctives lack natural order.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْعَكْسُ: قَلْبُ جُزْأَيِ الْقَضِيَّةِ مَعَ بَقَاءِ الصِّدْقِ. فَخَرَجَ بِـ طَرَفَيِ الْقَضِيَّةِ : تَبْدِيلُ السُّورِ مَثَلاً وَالْجِهَةِ، وَتَبْدِيلُ أَحَدِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ فَقَط ; كَقَوْلِكَ : «زَيْدٌ إِنْسَانٌ، زَيْدٌ عَالِمٌ». وَدَخَلَ فِي الْقَضِيَّةِ الْحَمْلِيَّةِ وَالْمُتَّصِلَةِ، وَأَمَّا الْمُنْفَصِلَةُ فَلَا عَكْسَ لَهَا، كَمَا سَيُنَبِّهُ عَلَيْهِ بِقَوْلِهِ : وَالْعَكْسُ فِي مُرَتَّبٍ بِالطَّبْعِ... الْبَيْتَ، وَهُوَ مُحْتَرَزُ التَّقْيِيدِ بِذَاتِ التَّرْتِيبِ الطَّبِيعِيِّ. وَقَوْلُنَا : بِعَيْنِ الآخَرِ مُخْرِجٌ لِعَكْسِ النَّقِيضِ بِنَوْعَيْهِ ; لِأَنَّ التَّبْدِيلَ فِيهِمَا كَمَا يَأْتِي لَيْسَ بِعَيْنِ كُلِّ مِنَ الطَّرَفَيْنِ. مَعْنَى التَّرْتِيبِ فِي ذَلِكَ : أَنَّ الْحَمْلِيَّةَ يَقْتَضِي الطَّبْعُ تَقْدِيمَ مَوْضُوعِهَا وَتَأْخِيرَ مَحْمُولِهَا ; إِذِ الثَّانِي مَسُوقٌ لِلْأَوَّلِ، وَتَعَقُّلُ الْمَسُوقِ ثَانٍ عَنْ تَعَقُّلِ الْمَسُوقِ لَهُ. وَالْمُتَّصِلَةُ أَيْضاً : يَقْتَضِي الطَّبْعُ تَقْدِيمَ مُقَدَّمِهَا وَتَأْخِيرَ تَالِيهَا ; لِأَنَّ الْأَوَّلَ طَالِبٌ لِلصُّحْبَةِ وَمَلْزُومٌ، وَالثَّانِي مَطْلُوبٌ لَهَا وَلَازِمٌ، وَتَعَقُّلُ الْمَلْزُومِ وَالطَّالِبِ سَابِقٌ عَلَى تَعَقُّلِ اللَّازِمِ وَالْمَطْلُوبِ. بِخِلَافِ الْمُنْفَصِلَةِ : فَتَرْتِيبُ طَرَفَيْهَا لَفْظِيٌّ فَقَطْ، فَلَكَ أَنْ تُقَدِّمَ وَتُؤَخِّرَ مَا شِئْتَ مِنْهُمَا، وَالْمَعْنَى بِحَالِهِ لَا يَتَبَدَّلُ; وَهَذَا الَّذِي ذَكَرَهُ وَهُوَ مُرْتَضَى الْمُحَقِّقِينَ، وَزَعَمَ الْقُطْبُ أَنَّهَا تَنْعَكِسُ ; لِأَنَّ الْحُكْمَ فِي نَحْوِ: «إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ الْعَدَدُ زَوْجاً، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ فَرْداً بِمُعَانَدَةِ الزَّوْجِيَّةِ لِلْفَرْدِيَّةِ، وَفِي عَكْسِهِ بِمُعَانَدَةِ الْفَرْدِيَّةِ لِلزَّوْجِيَّةِ، وَسَيَأْتِي لِلشَّارِحِ رَدُّهُ. ثُمَّ إِنَّ هَذَا الْقَيْدَ كَمَا يَخْرُجُ الْمُنْفَصِلَةَ كَمَا ذَكَرَ، يَخْرُجُ أَيْضاً الِاتِّفَاقِيَّةَ الْمُتَّصِلَةَ ; لِأَنَّ تَرْتِيبَ طَرَفَيْهَا لَفْظِيٌّ، لَا مَعْنَوِيٌّ ; نَبَّهَ عَلَى ذَلِكَ السَّعْدُ . قَوْلُهُ : (وَقَوْلُنَا : بِعَيْنِ الآخَرِ مُخْرِجٌ لِعَكْسِ النَّقِيضِ... إلخ) لَا يَحْتَاجُ لِهَذَا الْقَيْدِ ; لِأَنَّ عَكْسَ النَّقِيضِ بِنَوْعَيْهِ خَرَجَ بِإِضَافَةِ «قَلْبِ» لِ «جُزْأَيِ الْقَضِيَّةِ فِي كَلَامِ النَّاظِمِ لِعَدَمِ بَقَاءِ عَيْنِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ فِيهِمَا ; لِأَنَّ قَوْلَهُ : قَلْبُ جُزْأَيِ الْقَضِيَّةِ مُخْرِجٌ لِقَلْبِ غَيْرِ جُزْأَيِهَا، وَحِينَئِذٍ فَلَا يَحْتَاجُ إِلَى تَقْيِيدِ الْقَلْبِ بِالْعَيْنِ كَمَا فَعَلَ الشَّارِحُ. وَمُوجِبُ ذَلِكَ لَهُ : تَوَهُّمُ اتِّحَادِ التَّعْبِيرِ بِالتَّبْدِيلِ، كَمَا فَعَلَ صَاحِبُ «الْمُخْتَصَرِ» وَالتَّعْبِيرُ بِالْقَلْبِ كَمَا فَعَلَ النَّاظِمُ، وَالْفَرْقُ بَيْنَهُمَا كَمَا قَالَ شَيْخُنَا سَيِّدِي الطَّيِّبُ: أَبْيَنُ مِنْ أَنْ يُبَيَّنَ.
Conversion: turning over the two parts of the proposition while preserving truth. So it excludes changing the quantifier for example and the modality, and changing only one of the two parts, as in your saying: 'Zayd is a human, Zayd is knowledgeable.' And it includes the categorical and the conditional proposition. As for the disjunctive, it has no conversion, as he will point out by his saying: 'And conversion is in something ordered by nature...' the verse, and it is a restriction to the condition of natural order. And our saying: 'with the identical other' excludes conversion by contraposition of both types; because the substitution in them, as will come, is not with the identical each of the two parts. The meaning of order in that: the categorical requires by nature the precedence of its subject and the postponement of its predicate; since the second is driven for the first, and the understanding of the driven [second] comes after the understanding of that for which it is driven. And the conditional also requires by nature the precedence of its antecedent and the postponement of its consequent; because the first is a seeker of companionship and a [logical] ground, and the second is sought for it and a [logical] consequent, and the understanding of the ground and the seeker precedes the understanding of the consequent and the sought. Unlike the disjunctive: the ordering of its two parts is merely verbal, so you may put forward and delay whichever you wish of them, and the meaning remains unchanged. This is what he mentioned and it is the position of the verifiers. Al-Qutb claimed that it does convert, because the judgement in something like: 'Either the number is even or it is odd' is based on the opposition of evenness to oddness, and in its converse the opposition of oddness to evenness. The commentator will refute him. Then, this condition excludes the disjunctive as mentioned, and also excludes the accidental conditional; because the ordering of its two parts is verbal, not significative; al-Sa'd pointed that out. His saying: (And our saying: 'with the identical other' excludes conversion by contraposition... etc.) This condition is not needed; because conversion by contraposition of both types is already excluded by adding 'turning over' to 'the two parts of the proposition' in the poet's words, due to the non-preservation of the identical of the two parts in them; because his saying: 'turning over the two parts of the proposition' excludes turning over other than its two parts. Hence the restriction of turning over to 'the identical' as the commentator did is unnecessary. What necessitated that for him is the assumption that the expression with 'substitution' is the same as the expression with 'turning over', as the author of al-Mukhtasar did, while the poet used 'turning over'. The difference between them, as our master Sayyidi al-Tayyib said, is clearer than to be clarified.
Source pages p. 650

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of 'aks (conversion) in logic, refining the conditions. It begins by stating the accepted definition: 'turning over the two parts of the proposition while preserving truth.' Then it explains what this definition excludes: (1) changing only the quantifier or modality, (2) changing only one of the two parts (as in the example 'Zayd is a human, Zayd is knowledgeable' where the subject 'Zayd' is repeated but not swapped). The definition includes both categorical (subject-predicate) and conditional (if-then) propositions. Disjunctive propositions (either-or) cannot be converted because they lack natural order. The natural order is explained: in categoricals, the subject precedes the predicate by nature; in conditionals, the antecedent precedes the consequent; in disjunctives, the parts have only verbal order, so conversion is invalid. The text then discusses the phrase 'with the identical other' which excludes conversion by contraposition (where the parts are not identical after conversion). The commentator argues that this phrase is unnecessary because the poet's wording 'turning over the two parts' already implies the same parts. The confusion arises from equating 'tabdil' (substitution) with 'qalb' (inversion). This discussion shows the precision expected in defining logical terms.

Key Terms
العكس
قلب
الترتيب الطبيعي
عكس النقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does a disjunctive proposition (e.g., 'Either the number is even or odd') not admit conversion?
A) Because it has no truth value.
B) Because its two parts have only a verbal order, not a natural order.
C) Because it is not a valid proposition.
D) Because conversion would change the meaning.

Key Takeaway
The condition of natural order restricts conversion to categorical and conditional propositions only, excluding disjunctives and accidental conditionals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Examine the following propositions and determine whether they can be validly converted, giving a reason based on the condition of natural order: (1) 'Every intoxicant is prohibited' (categorical), (2) 'If it rains, then the ground is wet' (conditional), (3) 'Either an act is obligatory or forbidden' (disjunctive).

Immediate Inference
beginner

Simple Conversion (al-‘Aks al-Mustawi): Definition and Condition
Author defines simple conversion with examples and explains the condition of preserving truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the operation of simple conversion in categorical and conditional propositions, and evaluate whether a conversion satisfies the condition of preserving truth (baqa' al-sidq).

Core Idea
Simple conversion swaps parts and intentions; truth preservation is hypothetical, not actual.

In this chapter, the author defines simple conversion (al-‘aks al-mustawi) with clear examples from categorical and conditional propositions. He emphasizes that conversion is not just swapping terms mechanically; the logical intentions (intended for individuals or concept) also transfer. Moreover, the condition that the converse preserves truth does not require both propositions to be actually true, but rather that the converse is a necessary consequence of the original. This lesson will walk you through the definition, examples, and the key condition to help you recognize valid conversions in Islamic logical texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Determine whether the original proposition is categorical (subject-predicate) or conditional (antecedent-consequent). Look at the examples in the text: 'Every human is an animal' is categorical; 'Whenever something is human, it is animal' is conditional.
The type determines which parts are swapped.
2
Swap the parts
For categorical: exchange subject and predicate. For conditional: exchange antecedent and consequent. Write down the resulting statement. For 'Every human is animal', the converse is 'Some animal is human'.
Note the quantifier changes from universal to particular in the categorical example.
3
Track the intention shift
In the categorical example, 'human' was a subject intended for individuals, but in the converse it becomes a predicate intended for the concept. 'Animal' shifts from predicate intended for concept to subject intended for individuals. Verify that the intentions transfer correctly.
This is a key point: the logical roles and their intentions swap together.
4
Apply the truth preservation condition
Ask: If the original were true, would the converse necessarily be true? For 'Every human is animal', the converse 'Some animal is human' is true if the original is true. For an invalid conversion like 'Every animal is human', this condition fails.
The condition is hypothetical; it does not depend on actual truth.
5
Distinguish hypothetical from actual truth
The condition does not require both original and converse to be actually true. It requires that the converse is a necessary consequent of the original. This is why preservation of falsehood is not considered: a false original can have a true converse.
This addresses the common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ فِي الْعَكْسِ الْمُسْتَوِي. وَالْمُرَادُ بِـ «تَبْدِيلِهِمَا بِهِمَا: أَنْ يَجْعَلَ كُلٌّ مِنْهُمَا فِي مَرْتَبَةِ الْآخَرِ مَنْسُوخاً مِنْهُ حُكْمُ مَا نُقِلَ عَنْهُ مِنْ كَوْنِهِ مَوْضُوعاً أَوْ مَحْمُولاً، أَوْ مُقَدَّماً أَوْ تَالِياً، مُعْطَى حُكْمَ مَا نُقِلَ إِلَيْهِ مِنْ ذَلِكَ . فَإِذَا قُلْنَا مَثَلاً : كُلُّ إِنْسَانِ حَيَوَانٌ فَعَكْسُهُ بِالْمُسْتَوِي : بَعْضُ الْحَيَوَانِ مَوْضُوعاً مَقْصُوداً بِهِ الْأَفْرَادُ . وَإِذَا قُلْنَا فِي الْمُتَّصِلَةِ: كُلَّمَا كَانَ الشَّيْءُ إِنْسَاناً، كَانَ حَيَوَاناً فَعَكْسُهُ بِالْمُسْتَوِي : «قَدْ يَكُونُ إِذَا كَانَ الشَّيْءُ حَيَوَاناً كَانَ إِنْسَاناً، فَالْإِنْسَانُ كَانَ مَلْزُوماً فَصَارَ لَازِماً، وَالْحَيَوَانُ كَانَ لَازِماً فَصَارَ مَلْزُوماً، فَكُلُّ مِنَ الطَّرَفَيْنِ بَعْدَ التَّبْدِيلِ يَزُولُ عَنْهُ مَا كَانَ لَهُ قَبْلَهُ، وَيَصِيرُ لَهُ مَا كَانَ لِصَاحِبِهِ، وَلَيْسَ الْمُرَادُ أَنَّ الْمَوْضُوعَ يَصِيرُ مَحْمُولاً، وَهُوَ مُرَادٌ بِهِ الْأَفْرَادُ كَمَا كَانَ، وَالْمَحْمُولُ يَصِيرُ مَوْضُوعاً وَهُوَ مُرَادُ بِهِ الْمَفْهُومُ كَمَا كَانَ؛ لِأَنَّهُ خِلَافُ الْقَوَاعِدِ . وَقَوْلُهُ : (مَعَ بَقَاءِ الصِّدْقِ احْتَرَزَ بِهِ : مِنَ التَّبْدِيلِ الْمَذْكُورِ الَّذِي لَا يَبْقَى مَعَهُ الصِّدْقُ الْمَفْرُوضُ فِي الْأَصْلِ؛ كَقَوْلِنَا : كُلُّ حَيَوَانٍ إِنْسَانٌ فِي عَكْسِ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ»، وَلَيْسَ الْمُرَادُ بِـ بَقَاءِ الصِّدْقِ»: أَنَّ الْأَصْلَ وَالْعَكْسُ لَا بُدَّ أَنْ يَكُونَا صَادِقَيْنِ فِي الْوَاقِعِ، بَلِ الْمُرَادُ : أَنَّ الْأَصْلَ يَكُونُ بِحَيْثُ لَوْ فُرِضَ صِدْقُهُ لَزِمَ صِدْقُ الْعَكْسِ؛ قَالَهُ الْقُطْبُ . وَإِنَّمَا اعْتَبَرَ بَقَاءَ الصِّدْقِ وَلَمْ يَعْتَبِرْ بَقَاءَ الْكَذِبِ؛ لِأَنَّ الْعَكْسَ لَازِمٌ لِلْأَصْلِ، وَقَدْ عُلِمَ : أَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ مِنْ صِدْقِ الْمَلْزُومِ صِدْقُ لَازِمِهِ، وَلَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ كَذِبِهِ كَذِبُ لَازِمِهِ، فَقَوْلُنَا : ... قَصَارَة. قَوْلُهُ : (بِحَيْثُ لَوْ فُرِضَ صِدْقُهُ لَزِمَ صِدْقُ الْعَكْسِ) أَيْ : سَوَاءٌ كَانَ صَادِقاً فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ أَمْ لَا . قَوْلُهُ : (وَقَدْ عُلِمَ : أَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ مِنْ صِدْقِ الْمَلْزُومِ صِدْقُ لَازِمِهِ) أَيْ : وَإِلَّا لَلَزِمَ صِدْقُ الْمَلْزُومِ بِدُونِ اللَّازِمِ، وَهُوَ مُحَالٌ . قَوْلُهُ : (وَلَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ كَذِبِهِ كَذِبُ لَازِمِهِ ... إلخ) لِجَوَازِ أَنْ يَكُونَ الصَّادِقُ لَازِماً لِلْكَاذِبِ؛ إِذْ لَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ كَذِبِ الْمَلْزُومِ الْأَخَصِّ كَذِبُ اللَّازِمِ الْأَعَمِّ، وَالدَّلِيلُ عَلَى أَنَّهُ أَعَمُّ : صِدْقُهُ مَعَ كَذِبِ الْأَصْلِ كَالْمِثَالِ، وَشَرَطَهُ ابْنُ سِينَا فِي «النَّجَاةِ»؛ بِنَاءً عَلَى جَعْلِهِ الْعَكْسَ مِنَ اللَّازِمِ الْمُسَاوِي، فَلَا يُسَمَّى عِنْدَهُ فِي هَذَا الْقَوْلِ: عَكْساً إِلَّا مَا وَافَقَ فِي الصِّدْقِ وَالْكَذِبِ مَعاً، وَوَافَقَ فِي كِتَابِهِ «الشِّفَاءِ» الْجُمْهُورَ.
Chapter on simple conversion. What is meant by 'substituting them by each other' is that each of them is placed in the rank of the other, with the judgement of what was transferred from it of being subject or predicate, or antecedent or consequent, transferred to it, given the judgement of what was transferred to it from that. So when we say for example: 'Every human is an animal', its simple converse is: 'Some animal is human'. So 'human' was a subject intended for individuals, and became a predicate intended for the concept, and 'animal' was a predicate intended for the concept, and became a subject intended for individuals. And when we say in a conditional: 'Whenever something is a human, it is an animal', its simple converse is: 'Sometimes when something is an animal, it is a human'. So 'human' was a ground (malzum) and became a consequent (lazim), and 'animal' was a consequent and became a ground. So each of the two parts after substitution loses what it had before, and acquires what its counterpart had. And it is not meant that the subject becomes a predicate while still intended for individuals as before, and the predicate becomes a subject while still intended for the concept as before; because that contradicts the rules. And his saying: 'with preservation of truth' is a precaution against the mentioned substitution with which the truth assumed in the original does not remain; like our saying: 'Every animal is a human' as the converse of 'Every human is an animal'. And what is meant by 'preservation of truth' is not that the original and the converse must both be true in reality, but rather that the original is such that if it were assumed true, the truth of the converse would follow; al-Qutb said that. And he only considered preservation of truth and not preservation of falsehood, because the converse is a necessary consequent (lazim) of the original, and it is known that from the truth of the ground (malzum) the truth of its consequent follows, but from the falsehood of the ground the falsehood of its consequent does not necessarily follow. So our saying: ... His saying: (such that if its truth were assumed, the truth of the converse would follow) meaning: whether it is actually true or not. His saying: (And it is known that from the truth of the ground the truth of its consequent follows) meaning: otherwise the truth of the ground would follow without the consequent, which is impossible. His saying: (And from its falsehood the falsehood of its consequent does not necessarily follow... etc.) because it is possible that the true is a consequent of the false; for from the falsehood of the more specific ground the falsehood of the more general consequent does not follow. The proof that it is more general is its truth together with the falsehood of the original, as in the example. Ibn Sina stipulated in al-Najat, based on his making the converse from the equivalent consequent, so according to him in this statement, nothing is called 'converse' except what agrees in truth and falsehood together. But in his book al-Shifa he agreed with the majority.
Source pages p. 651

Commentary
This passage is a chapter on simple conversion (al-‘aks al-mustawi), explaining its meaning and conditions. It defines 'substituting them by each other' as each part taking the other's logical role (subject/predicate or antecedent/consequent) and inheriting its corresponding judgement. An example from categoricals: 'Every human is an animal' converts to 'Some animal is human', where the logical intention changes: 'human' was intended as a subject for individuals but becomes a predicate for the concept; 'animal' was a predicate for concept but becomes a subject for individuals. Similarly, a conditional example: 'Whenever something is a human, it is an animal' converts to 'Sometimes when something is an animal, it is a human', swapping the roles of ground and consequent. The text clarifies that it is not the same parts with the same intention; that would violate the rules of logic. Then it discusses the condition 'with preservation of truth'. This does not require actual truth of both original and converse; rather, it is a hypothetical condition: if the original were true, the converse must be true. This is because the converse is a necessary consequent (lazim) of the original. The text explains that truth of the ground implies truth of the consequent, but falsehood of the ground does not imply falsehood of the consequent (since the same consequent can follow from false premises). It then mentions Ibn Sina's stricter requirement in al-Najat that the converse must be equivalent (same truth value), but notes that in al-Shifa he agrees with the majority (the hypothetical condition). This passage is an expansion of the definition of simple conversion, clarifying both the transformation of logical roles and the nature of truth preservation.

Key Terms
العكس المستوي (al-‘aks al-mustawi) – simple conversion
بقاء الصدق (baqa’ al-sidq) – preservation of truth
لازم (lazim) – necessary consequent
ملزوم (malzum) – ground

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the passage, what does 'preservation of truth' (baqa' al-sidq) mean for simple conversion?
A) The original and converses must both be true.
B) If the original were true, the converse would necessarily be true.
C) The original must be false.
D) The converse must preserve the same truth value as the original in all cases.

Key Takeaway
Simple conversion swaps the logical parts and their intentions, and the key condition is that the converse must be a necessary consequent of the original, preserving truth in a hypothetical sense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Convert the categorical proposition 'Every nabidh (date drink) is intoxicant' to its simple converse. Explain whether the condition of truth preservation is satisfied and why.
