Direct Converse: Definition and Intention Shift
Definition of 'aks mustawi with swapping explanation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'aks mustawi and correctly perform the conversion operation on categorical and conditional propositions while identifying the shift in intention (individuals vs. concept).

Core Idea
In direct converse, terms swap positions and intended reference shifts from individuals to concept.

The operation of 'aks mustawi is a fundamental tool in Islamic logic for transforming propositions. It allows you to derive a new proposition by swapping the two parts. But this swap is not mechanical—it also involves a change in how the terms are intended: as subjects or predicates, their reference changes from particular individuals to universal concepts. In this lesson, you will learn the precise definition and see it applied to categorical and conditional propositions, guided by the classical example from human and animal.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the definition
Read the Arabic definition: 'المراد بتبديلهما بهما أن يجعل كل منهما في مرتبة الآخر منسوخا منه حكم ما نقل عنه من كونه موضوعا أو محمولا أو مقدما أو تاليا، معطى حكم ما نقل إليه من ذلك.' The key is that each piece loses its previous judgment and gains the judgment of the other position.
2
Analyze the categorical example
In 'كل إنسان حيوان', 'إنسان' was subject intended by its individuals (particular humans). After conversion to 'بعض الحيوان إنسان', it becomes predicate intended by its concept (the universal meaning of human). Similarly, 'حيوان' shifts from predicate intended by concept to subject intended by individuals.
This illustrates that the intention (قصد) of the term changes with its new role.
3
Analyze the conditional example
In the conditional 'كلما كان الشيء إنسانا كان حيوانا', 'إنسان' was antecedent (ملزوم) and becomes consequent (لازم). 'حيوان' shifts from consequent to antecedent. The intention change is analogous: each term acquires the judgment of its new position.
4
Avoid the common mistake
The author emphasizes that it is not meant that the subject becomes a predicate still intended by individuals, nor the predicate becomes a subject still intended by concept. That would violate the rules. The intention must shift appropriately.
This precision prevents a misunderstanding of the operation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في العكس المستوي
وَالْمُرَادُ بِـ «تَبْدِيلِهِمَا بِهِمَا: أَنْ يَجْعَلَ كُلٌّ مِنْهُمَا فِي مَرْتَبَةِ الآخَرِ مَنْسُوخاً مِنْهُ حُكْمُ مَا نُقِلَ عَنْهُ مِنْ كَوْنِهِ مَوْضُوعاً أَوْ مَحْمُولاً ، أَوْ مُقَدَّماً أَوْ تَالِياً ، مُعْطَى حُكْمَ مَا نُقِلَ إِلَيْهِ مِنْ ذَلِكَ .
فَإِذَا قُلْنَا مَثَلاً : كُلُّ إِنْسَانِ حَيَوَانٌ فَعَكْسُهُ بِالمُسْتَوِي : بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ إِنْسَانٌ»، فَالإِنْسَانُ كَانَ مَوْضُوعاً مَقْصُوداً بِهِ الأَفْرَادُ، فَصَارَ مَحْمُولاً مَقْصُوداً بِهِ المَفْهُومُ، وَالحَيَوَانُ كَانَ مَحْمُولاً مَقْصُوداً بِهِ المَفْهُومُ ، فَصَارَ مَوْضُوعاً مَقْصُوداً بِهِ الْأَفْرَادُ .
وَإِذَا قُلْنَا فِي المُتَّصِلَةِ: كُلَّمَا كَانَ الشَّيْءُ إِنْسَاناً ، كَانَ حَيَوَاناً فَعَكْسُهُ بِالْمُسْتَوِي : «قَدْ يَكُونُ إِذَا كَانَ الشَّيْءُ حَيَوَاناً كَانَ إِنْسَاناً ، فَالإِنْسَانُ كَانَ مَلْزُوماً فَصَارَ لَازماً ، وَالحَيَوَانُ كَانَ لَازِماً فَصَارَ مَلْزُوماً ، فَكُلُّ مِنَ الطَّرَفَيْنِ بَعْدَ التَّبْدِيلِ يَزُولُ عَنْهُ مَا كَانَ لَهُ قَبْلَهُ، وَيَصِيرُ لَهُ مَا كَانَ لِصَاحِبِهِ، وَلَيْسَ المُرَادُ أَنَّ المَوْضُوعَ يَصِيرُ مَحْمُولاً، وَهُوَ مُرَادٌ بِهِ الأَفْرَادُ كَمَا كَانَ، وَالمَحْمُولُ يَصِيرُ مَوْضُوعاً وَهُوَ مُرَادُ بِهِ المَفْهُومُ كَمَا كَانَ؛ لِأَنَّهُ خِلَافُ القَوَاعِدِ .
Chapter on the direct converse. What is meant by 'exchanging them with them' is that each of them is placed in the rank of the other, with the judgment of what was transferred from it being removed (whether it was subject or predicate, or antecedent or consequent), and is given the judgment of what was transferred to it. For example, if we say: 'Every human is an animal', its direct converse is 'Some animal is a human'. So 'human' was a subject (intended by its individuals) and becomes a predicate (intended by its concept), and 'animal' was a predicate (intended by its concept) and becomes a subject (intended by its individuals). And if we say in the conditional: 'Whenever something is a human, it is an animal', its direct converse is 'Sometimes when something is an animal, it is a human'. So 'human' was an antecedent and becomes a consequent, and 'animal' was a consequent and becomes an antecedent. So each of the two sides, after the exchange, loses what it had before and acquires what belonged to the other. It is not meant that the subject becomes a predicate, intended by individuals as it was, and the predicate becomes a subject, intended by concept as it was; because that is contrary to the rules.
Source pages p. 651

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of 'aks mustawi (direct converse). The author first defines it as the operation of exchanging the two parts of a proposition (subject/predicate or antecedent/consequent) such that each takes the position and judgment of the other. He then illustrates with two examples: a categorical proposition (Every human is an animal → Some animal is a human) and a conditional proposition (Whenever something is human, it is animal → Sometimes when something is animal, it is human). In the categorical example, he notes that the subject 'human' was intended by its individuals (meaning particular humans) but in the converse it becomes a predicate intended by its concept (the universal meaning). Similarly, the predicate 'animal' was intended by its concept and becomes a subject intended by its individuals. The same shift occurs in the conditional: the antecedent 'human' becomes a consequent, and vice versa. Importantly, the author clarifies that the swap does not mean that the subject literally becomes a predicate with the same intention (individuals) and the predicate becomes a subject with the same intention (concept); that would violate the rules of logic. Instead, the intended meaning of each term changes according to its new position. This precision guards against a common misunderstanding.

Key Terms
العكس المستوي
تبديل
موضوع
محمول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example 'كل إنسان حيوان', what becomes of the subject 'إنسان' in the converse 'بعض الحيوان إنسان'?
A) It remains subject intended by its individuals.
B) It becomes predicate intended by its concept.
C) It becomes predicate intended by its individuals.
D) It becomes subject intended by its concept.

Key Takeaway
The direct converse swaps subject/predicate or antecedent/consequent while simultaneously shifting the intended reference from individuals to concept or vice versa.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Given the proposition 'كل نجس يجب تطهيره' (Every impure thing must be purified), perform the direct converse and state the resulting proposition. Then explain how the intention of the terms shifts (individuals vs. concept).
?
2. In a conditional: 'إذا كان الشيء خمرا فهو محرم' (If something is khamr, it is forbidden), perform the direct converse and show the swapping of antecedent/consequent and the corresponding change in intention.

Preservation of Truth in Immediate Converse
Explanation of truth preservation condition in 'aks

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the logical condition of truth preservation from actual truth in immediate converse, and apply it to determine valid converses.

Core Idea
Preservation of truth in conversion is about logical implication, not factual correspondence.

In this passage, the author clarifies a subtle but crucial point about the condition 'preservation of truth' (بقاء الصدق) in the definition of immediate converse (العكس المستوي). Many beginners mistakenly think that both the original and the converse must be actually true in the world. The author corrects this: the condition is about logical necessity—if the original were true, the converse must be true. This distinction is essential for correctly applying conversion in Islamic logical arguments without confusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
Read the first part of the passage. The condition 'preservation of truth' is meant to exclude invalid exchanges like 'Every animal is a human' from the true original 'Every human is an animal'. Here the original is true, but the converse is false. So what exactly does 'preservation of truth' require?
The example shows that the truth of the original alone is not enough; the converse must logically follow.
2
Distinguish actual truth from logical implication
The author quotes al-Qutb: preservation of truth does not mean that both are true in reality. It means that the original is such that if it were true, the converse would be true. For instance, 'Every human is an animal' is true, and its converse 'Every animal is a human' is false, so the condition fails. But consider a false original like 'Every animal is a human'; its converse 'Some human is an animal' is true. Here the original is false and the converse is true. The condition looks only at the implication from a hypothetical true original.
The condition is about the relationship between the propositions, not their actual truth values.
3
Analyse the luzum (entailment) principle
The passage explains why truth (ṣidq) is considered rather than falsehood (kidhb). Because the converse is a necessary consequent (lāzim) of the original (malzūm). It is a known principle that from the truth of the antecedent, the truth of its necessary consequent follows, but from the falsehood of the antecedent, the falsehood of the consequent does not necessarily follow.
This mirrors the logical principle: truth cascades forward, falsehood does not necessarily cascade.
4
Apply the condition to evaluate examples
Take the example from the text: Original: 'Every human is an animal' (true). Converse: 'Every animal is a human' (false). Since the original is true but the converse is false, the condition of preservation of truth is violated. Now consider: Original: 'No wine is permissible' (true). Its simple converse: 'No permissible is wine' (true). Here the condition holds because if the original is true, the converse is also true.
Use the test: assume the original is true; does the converse necessarily become true?
5
Recognise the stricter view of Ibn Sina
The marginal note mentions that Ibn Sina in al-Najat required the converse to be coextensive (musāwī), meaning it must agree in truth value. According to him, only when both are true or both false does he call it a 'aks. However, he later aligned with the majority in al-Shifā. The majority view (including the author) is that the condition is only one-way implication.
This shows that logical conditions can be subject to refinement among scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And his statement: 'with preservation of truth' guards against the mentioned exchange that does not preserve the assumed truth in the original; like our saying: 'Every animal is a human' in converse of 'Every human is an animal'. And what is meant by 'preservation of truth' is not that the original and converse must both be true in reality, but rather that the original is such that if it were true, the converse would be true; said by al-Qutb. And he considered preservation of truth and not preservation of falsehood because the converse is a necessary concomitant of the original, and it is known that from the truth of the antecedent the truth of its necessary consequent follows, and it does not follow from its falsehood the falsehood of its consequent. So our saying: ... [Marginal note: His statement: 'such that if its truth were supposed, the truth of the converse would follow', i.e., whether it is true in itself or not. His statement: 'And it is known that from the truth of the antecedent the truth of its necessary consequent follows', i.e., otherwise the truth of the antecedent without the consequent would be necessary, which is impossible. His statement: 'And it does not follow from its falsehood the falsehood of its consequent... etc.' because it is possible for the true to be a consequent of the false; since from the falsehood of the more specific antecedent the falsehood of the more general consequent does not follow, and the proof that it is more general is its truth with the falsehood of the original, as in the example. Ibn Sina stipulated in al-Najat, based on his making the converse a necessarily equal consequent, so according to him in this statement, nothing is called a converse except that which agrees in truth and falsehood together, and he agreed with the majority in his book al-Shifa.]
Source pages p. 651

Commentary
This passage explains the condition 'preservation of truth' (بقاء الصدق) in the definition of 'aks mustawi. The author clarifies that this condition excludes a swap that does not preserve the truth assumed in the original. For example, if we take the true statement 'Every human is an animal' and swap it to 'Every animal is a human', this is not a valid converse because the truth is not preserved (the latter is false). The truth condition does not mean that both the original and the converse must be actually true in reality. Rather, it means that the original proposition must be such that if it were true, the converse would necessarily be true. This is a logical entailment, not a requirement of actual truth. The author cites al-Qutb for this view. He then explains why truth (sidq) is considered rather than falsehood (kidhb): because the converse is a necessary consequent (lazim) of the original. It is a logical principle that from the truth of the antecedent (malzum) the truth of its necessary consequent follows, but from the falsehood of the antecedent the falsehood of the consequent does not necessarily follow. The marginal notes elaborate: if the original were false, the converse could still be true, as in the example 'Every animal is a human' (false) but its converse 'Some human is an animal' (true). This establishes that the condition is about logical implication, not factual correspondence. The note also mentions Ibn Sina's stricter view in al-Najat, where the converse must be coextensive (musawi) and thus agree in truth value, but he later aligned with the majority in al-Shifa.

لزوم
ملزوم
لازم

A) Both original and converse must be actually true in reality.
B) If the original were true, the converse must be true.
C) The converse must be true in reality regardless of the original.
D) The original must be true and the converse must be false.

Key Takeaway
The condition of truth preservation in converse is about logical entailment, not actual truth: it requires that if the original is true, the converse follows necessarily.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Immediate Inference
intermediate
8 min
Conditions of Preserving Quality and Necessity in Immediate Conversion
Author stipulates quality and necessity conditions for valid conversion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a candidate converse preserves quality and necessity, and determine why a universal affirmative converts only particularly, not universally.

Core Idea
A valid converse must preserve quality and follow necessarily—not just coincidentally.

We have learned that immediate conversion (al-ʿaks al-mustawī) swaps subject and predicate. But not every swap is valid. Logicians stipulate that the converse must preserve the original’s quality (affirmative/negative) and must follow necessarily (luzūm) from the original. This lesson examines these two conditions with the classic example of the universal affirmative, which only converts particularly unless the terms are coextensive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition and its quality
Determine whether the original proposition is affirmative or negative. The quality (kayfiyya) must be preserved in the converse.
2
Check if the candidate converse preserves quality
If the original is affirmative, the converse must be affirmative; if negative, negative. For example, 'Every human is an animal' cannot be converted to 'Some animal is not a human' because that changes affirmative to negative.
The passage explicitly rejects such a swap as not a valid converse.
3
Check if the converse follows necessarily, not coincidentally
The truth of the converse must be a necessary consequence of the original, not an accidental truth due to coextensive terms. For example, 'Every human is rational' converts to 'Every rational is human', but this is coincidental because the terms are equal in extension.
If the predicate is more general, the universal converse fails, proving necessity is not inherent.
4
Test the universal affirmative example
Apply to 'Every human is an animal' (predicate more general). Its valid converse is 'Some animal is human' (particular affirmative). Universal converse would be false.
The author states: 'therefore the universal affirmative does not convert as itself'.
5
Determine the general rule
Universal affirmative propositions convert only as particulars, because the predicate is usually more general. Even when coextensive, the universal converse is coincidental, not necessary.
This rule is derived from the condition of necessity and the nature of the universal affirmative form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كُلُّ حَيَوَانٍ إِنْسَانٌ كَاذِبٌ مَعَ صِدْقِ عَكْسِهِ، وَهُوَ قَوْلُنَا : بَعْضُ الإِنْسَانِ حَيَوَانٌ . وَقَوْلُهُ : (وَالكَيْفِيَّة) أي : الإِيجَابُ وَالسَّلْبُ يُخْرِجُ : التَّبْدِيلَ الَّذِي لَمْ يَبْقَ مَعَهُ الكَيْفُ ؛ كَمَا لَوْ قُلْتَ فِي عَكْسِ كُلُّ إِنْسَانِ حَيَوَانٌ»: «لَيْسَ بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ إِنْسَاناً»، فَلَا يُسَمَّى عَكْساً فِي العُرْفِ.
وَقَوْلُنَا : عَلَى وَجْهِ اللُّزُومِ يُخْرِجُ : التَّبْدِيلَ المَذْكُورِ إِذَا بَقِيَ مَعَهُ الصِّدْقُ وَالكَيْفُ
عَلَى وَجْهِ الاِتِّفَاقِ دُونَ لُزُوم ; كَقَوْلِنَا : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ نَاطِقٌ، فَإِنَّهُ يَصْدُقُ مَعَهُ كُلُّ نَاطِقٍ إِنْسَانُ، لَكِنْ صِدْقُ الكُلّيّةِ المُوجَبَةِ فِي عَكْسِ مِثْلِهَا إِنَّمَا هُوَ اتِّفَاقِيُّ ; لِخُصُوص (۱) المَادَّةِ ; أَعْنِي: كَوْنَ المَحْمُولِ مُسَاوِياً لِلمَوْضُوع، وَلَيْسَ لَازِماً لِصُورَةِ الكُلِّيَّةِ المُوْجَبَةِ مِنْ حَيْثُ هِيَ ; بِدَلِيلِ تَخَلْفِهِ، حَيْثُ يَكُونُ المَحْمُولُ أَعَمَّ فِي قَوْلِكَ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ»، فَإِنَّ عَكْسَهُ لَا يَصْدُقُ كُلِّيَّةٌ، بَلْ جُزْئِيَّةٌ ، فَلِهَذَا كَانَتِ الكُلِّيَّةُ المُوجَبَةُ لَا تَنْعَكِسُ كَنَفْسِهَا ; لِعَدَمِ اطْرَادِ صِدْقِهِ حِينَئِذٍ، وَلِهَذَا اسْتَثْنَى المُصَنِّفُ هَذِهِ الصُّورَةَ مِنِ اشْتِرَاطِ بَقَاءِ الكَم بِقَوْلِهِ

قوله : (أي: الإِيجَابُ وَالسَّلْبُ) أي: فإن كان الأصل موجباً كان العكس موجباً، وإن كان سالباً كان سالباً، وذلك لأنَّ العكس من لوازم الأصل، والموجب قد يتخلف وبالعكس أي : في قولنا : كلُّ إنسان ناطق لا يصدق العكس سالباً ; أعني قولنا : «بعض الناطق ليس بإنسان»، وفي نحو قولنا : «لا شيء مِنَ الإنسان بفرس لا يصدق العكس موجباً; أعني قولنا : بعض الفرس إنسان، فاللازم المنضبط هو الموافق في الكيف. قوله : (عَلَى وَجْهِ اللُّزُومِ) قيد لا بدَّ منه بقي على الناظم، وأحسنُ مِن تعريف النَّاظم قول

وقول شيخنا في خريدته :
وَهُوَ قَلْبُ طَرَفَيْ قَضِيَّه تَرْتِيبُهَا بِالطَّبْعِ لَا الوَضْعِيَّه مَعَ بَقَاءِ الكَيْفِ وَالصِّدْقِ بِلَا تَخَلُفٍ، وَمَا إِلَيْهِ حُوِّلًا
(۱) في (أ) : «في خُصُوص» بدلاً من لِخُصُوص».
Every animal is a human is false while its converse is true, namely our saying: 'Some human is an animal'. And his statement: 'and the quality', i.e. affirmation and negation, excludes the exchange that does not preserve the quality; as if you say in the converse of 'Every human is an animal': 'Some animal is not a human', then it is not called a converse in convention. And our statement: 'as a necessary consequence' excludes the mentioned exchange when truth and quality are preserved but as a coincidence not necessary; like our saying: 'Every human is rational', for its converse 'Every rational is human' is true, but the truth of the universal affirmative in the converse of its like is only coincidental, due to the particularity of the matter, i.e., the predicate being equal to the subject, and it is not necessary for the form of the universal affirmative as such, as evidenced by its failure when the predicate is more general in your saying: 'Every human is an animal', for its converse is not true as universal but as particular. Therefore the universal affirmative does not convert as itself, because its truth is not consistent in that case, and for this reason the author excluded this form from the condition of preserving quantity by his saying... [Marginal note: His statement: 'i.e., affirmation and negation' means that if the original is affirmative, the converse is affirmative; if negative, then negative. That is because the converse is a necessary concomitant of the original, but the affirmative may fail and vice versa, as in our saying: 'Every human is rational' does not hold as a negative converse, i.e., 'Some rational is not human', and in our saying: 'Nothing of human is a horse' does not hold as an affirmative converse, i.e., 'Some horse is human'. So the controlled necessary concomitant is the one that matches in quality. His statement: 'as a necessary consequence' is a necessary condition that the author (al-Nazim) omitted. And better than the author's definition is the saying of the author of al-Qadiriyya: 'The direct converse: exchanging each naturally ordered term with another, fulfilling with preservation of truth and quality as a necessary consequence, after which it is obtained.' And our teacher's saying in his Khari'da: 'It is the reversal of the two terms of a proposition whose order is natural, not conventional, with preservation of quality and truth without failure, and that to which it is converted.']
Source pages p. 652

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of the conditions for a valid 'aks mustawi, focusing on the preservation of quality (كيفية) and necessity (لزوم). The author begins by illustrating an invalid converse that fails to preserve quality: if we take 'Every human is an animal' and convert it to 'Some animal is not a human', this changes the quality from affirmative to negative, so it is not a proper converse. Next, he addresses the condition of necessity: the converse must follow necessarily from the original, not merely by accident. He gives the example 'Every human is rational' (كل إنسان ناطق). Here, the converse 'Every rational is human' is true, but this truth is coincidental because it depends on the particular matter (i.e., that 'rational' is coextensive with 'human'). It is not a necessary logical consequence of the form of a universal affirmative proposition. The proof is that when the predicate is more general (e.g., 'animal' is more general than 'human'), the universal affirmative does not convert as universal—its converse is only particular ('Some animal is human'). Therefore, universal affirmative propositions do not convert universally as such; they only convert as particulars. The author notes that the original writer (al-Nazim) excepted this form from the condition of preserving quantity (كم). The marginal notes add: the quality condition means that if the original is affirmative, the converse must be affirmative; if negative, negative. The converse is a necessary concomitant, but the controlled one is that which agrees in quality. Then the note praises two poetic definitions of 'aks mustawi from other scholars, which incorporate all conditions: natural order, preservation of truth and quality, and necessity.

Key Terms
كيف (kayf): quality of a proposition (affirmation or negation).
لزوم (luzūm): necessary consequence.
كلية موجبة (kulliyya mūjaba): universal affirmative proposition.
اتفاقي (ittifāqī): coincidental; truth that is not necessary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because 'animal' is more general than 'human', so the converse can only be particular.
B) Because conversion always changes the quantity.
C) Because the original is false.
D) Because the condition of necessity prevents it.

Key Takeaway
The universal affirmative does not convert universally; it converts only particularly, because the predicate is often more general, and even when coextensive, the universal converse is coincidental, not necessary.

The Three Conditions of 'Aks Mustawi
Defines 'aks mustawi with conditions of quality, truth, and necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three conditions of 'aks mustawi and determine whether a given conversion satisfies them.

Core Idea
Simple conversion preserves truth only if quality and necessity hold.

In this lesson, we examine the definition of 'aks mustawi (simple conversion), the fundamental immediate inference in Islamic logic. We will break down the three necessary conditions — quality, truth, and necessity — and see how they distinguish a valid conversion from a mere accidental swapping of terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the operation 'aks mustawi
Recognize that conversion exchanges subject and predicate while preserving the original proposition's truth. The order must be natural (subject becomes predicate).
The term 'mustawi' indicates no change in quantity or quality beyond what is required.
2
Extract the condition of quality
The converse must have the same quality (affirmative or negative) as the original. Changing quality, e.g., from affirmative to negative, invalidates the conversion.
The text stresses that the converse is a necessary concomitant; differing quality would break the logical link.
3
Extract the condition of truth
The converse must be true if the original is true. But truth alone is insufficient; it must be necessary, not accidental.
4
Extract the condition of necessity
The converse must follow necessarily from the original, not merely happen to be true due to special circumstances (like co-extension of terms).
The condition of necessity is key to avoid fallacious conversions that are only accidentally true.
5
Apply the conditions to examples
Test examples: 'Every human is animal' converts only to 'Some animal is human' (not universal) because necessity fails for universal; 'Every human is rational' accidentally converts universally because 'rational' and 'human' are co-extensive, but this is not necessary for the form.
Understand why the universal affirmative (A) does not convert as a universal (A) as a rule.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله: (والكيفية) أي: الإيجاب والسلبيخرج التبديل الذي لم يبق معه الكيف كما لو قلت في عكس كل إنسان حيوان: ليس بعض الحيوان إنسانا فلا يسمى عكسا في العرف. وقولنا: على وجه اللزوم يخرج التبديل المذكور إذا بقي معه الصدق والكيف على وجه الاتفاق دون لزوم كقولنا: كل إنسان ناطق فإنه يصدق معه كل ناطق إنسان لكن صدق الكلية الموجبة في عكس مثلها إنما هو اتفاقي لخصوص المادة أعني كون المحمول مساويا للموضوع وليس لازما لصورة الكلية الموجبة من حيث هي بدليل تخلقه حيث يكون المحمول أعم في قولك كل إنسان حيوان فإن عكسه لا يصدق كلية بل جزئية فلهذا كانت الكلية الموجبة لا تنعكس كنفسها لعدم اطراد صدقه حينئذ ولهذا استثنى المصنف هذه الصورة من اشتراط بقاء الكم بقوله. قوله: (أي: الإيجاب والسلبي) أي فإن كان الأصل موجبا كان العكس موجبا وإن كان سالبا كان سالبا وذلك لأن العكس من لوازم الأصل والموجب قد يتخلف وبالعكس أي في قولنا: كل إنسان ناطق لا يصدق العكس سالبا أعني قولنا: بعض الناطق ليس بإنسان وفي نحو قولنا: لا شيء من الإنسان بفرس لا يصدق العكس موجبا أعني قولنا: بعض الفرس إنسان فاللازم المنضبط هو الموافق في الكيف. قوله: (على وجه اللزوم) قيد لا بد منه بقي على الناظم وأحسن من تعريف الناظم قول صاحب القادرية: المستوي تبديل كل طرف مرتب طبعا بآخر يفي مع بقاء الصدق والكيف على وجه اللزوم بعده محصلا وقول شيخنا في خريدته: وهو قلب طرفي قضية ترتيبها بالطبع لا الوضعية مع بقاء الكيف والصدق بلا تخلف وما إليه حولا
And His saying: (And the quality) meaning: affirmation and negation excludes the substitution in which the quality does not remain, as when you say in the converse of 'Every human is an animal': 'Not some animal is a human', so it is not called a converse in convention. And our saying: 'on the basis of necessity' excludes the aforementioned substitution when truth and quality remain accidentally without necessity, like our saying: 'Every human is rational', for with it 'Every rational is human' is true, but the truth of the universal affirmative in the converse of its like is only accidental due to the specificity of the material, i.e., the predicate being co-extensive with the subject, and it is not necessary for the form of the universal affirmative as such, as evidenced by its failure when the predicate is more general in your saying 'Every human is an animal', for its converse is not true universally but particularly. Therefore the universal affirmative does not convert as itself because its truth is not regular at that time, hence the author excepted this form from the condition of preserving quantity by his saying. His saying: (i.e., affirmation and negation) i.e., if the original is affirmative then the converse is affirmative, and if it is negative then it is negative, because the converse is a necessary concomitant of the original, and the affirmative may fail and vice versa; i.e., in our saying 'Every human is rational' the converse is not true as negative, meaning our saying 'Some rational is not human', and in like our saying 'Nothing of human is a horse' the converse is not true as affirmative, meaning our saying 'Some horse is human', so the consistent necessary concomitant is the one that agrees in quality. His saying: (on the basis of necessity) a necessary restriction omitted by the versifier, and better than the versifier's definition is the saying of the author of al-Qadiriyya: 'The simple is the exchange of each term naturally ordered for another that fulfills with the preservation of truth and quality on the basis of necessity afterward, achieved.' And the saying of our shaykh in his Khurida: 'It is the inversion of the two terms of a proposition whose ordering is natural, not conventional, with preservation of quality and truth without failure, and what is turned around it.'
Source pages p. 652

Commentary
This passage defines 'aks mustawi (simple conversion) and establishes its three conditions: preservation of quality, truth, and necessity. The commentary begins by explaining the condition of quality: the converse must have the same quality (affirmative or negative) as the original. If you change quality, e.g., converting 'Every human is an animal' to 'Some animal is not human,' this is not called a converse in technical usage. Next, the condition of necessity: the converse must follow necessarily from the original, not merely accidentally. For example, 'Every human is rational' converts to 'Every rational is human' — but this universal affirmative converse is only accidentally true because 'rational' and 'human' are co-extensive. It is not necessary for the form of universal affirmative as such, as proven by cases where the predicate is broader, e.g., 'Every human is an animal' converts only to 'Some animal is human.' Thus, the universal affirmative does not convert as itself (i.e., as a universal). The author of the matn excepted this from the requirement of preserving quantity. Then the commentary clarifies the condition of quality further: since the converse is a necessary concomitant of the original, the consistent necessity is agreement in quality; examples illustrate that switching quality fails. Finally, two alternative definitions are provided: one from the author of al-Qadiriyya, emphasizing the exchange of naturally ordered terms with preservation of truth, quality, and necessity; another from the commentator's shaykh, emphasizing inversion of terms with natural ordering and preservation without failure.

الكيفية
اللزوم
المساوي

B) Because the condition of quality is violated in the latter.

Key Takeaway

Universal Affirmative Exception in Conversion
Refutes Ibn Harun's exception for universal affirmative conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct converse of a universal affirmative proposition and explain why Ibn Harun's exceptional view is rejected.

Core Idea
Universal affirmative never converts as universal; only particular affirmative is guaranteed.

The universal affirmative proposition is the exception in simple conversion: it cannot be converted to itself universally. Some logicians like Ibn Harun argued for an exception when the predicate is co-extensive, but the majority rejected this because logical rules must hold across all materials, not just special cases. In this lesson, we'll examine this critical refinement and learn the correct conversion rule.

Guided Path
1
Understand simple conversion
Simple conversion (al-'aks al-mustawi) means swapping subject and predicate while preserving truth and quality. For example, 'Every human is an animal' becomes 'Some animal is human'.
The quantity may change.
2
Apply conversion to universal affirmative
Take 'Every S is P'. If you try 'Every P is S', it fails because not all P may be S. For instance, 'Every dog is an animal' does not imply 'Every animal is a dog'. So universal affirmative converts only to particular affirmative: 'Some P is S'.
This is the standard rule.
3
Consider Ibn Harun's exception
Ibn Harun claimed that if the predicate is co-extensive with the subject (e.g., 'Every human is a rational animal'), then the converse can be universal because the terms are equal in extension.
His view is based on material coincidence, not formal logical rule.
4
Understand the majority's rejection
The majority (al-jumhūr) argue that a logical rule must be regular (muṭṭarid) across all materials, independent of content. Since the exception only occurs in special cases, it is not a valid general rule.
The commentator refines: regularity means independence from material, not mere consistency in some cases.
5
Confirm the correct converse
Thus, the only universally valid converse of a universal affirmative is a particular affirmative: from 'Every S is P' deduce 'Some P is S'.
This holds always, regardless of material.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في العكس المستوي إلا الموجب الكلية... إلخ، وما اختاره ابن هارون من التفصيل فيها؛ أي: إنها تنعكس كنفسها إن كان المحمول مساويا، وتنعكس جزئية إذا كان أعم مخالف للجمهور؛ لأنهم لا يعتبرون من الأحكام إلا ما كان مطردا في جميع المواد. وظاهر المصنف: أن القضايا كلها تنعكس كنفسها غير الكلية الموجبة، وليس على إطلاقه، فقد علمت أن أصول القضايا أربع: كلية، وجزئية، ومهملة، وشخصية؛ وكلها: موجبة وسالبة، فالمجموع ثمانية. فأما الموجبات الأربع فعكسها كلها بالمستوي جزئية موجبة، فالكلية كما تقدم، وإذا قلت في الجزئية: بعض الأبيض حيوان فعكسه: بعض الحيوان أبيض، وإذا قلت في المهملة: الجسم حيوان فعكسه: بعض الحيوان جسم، وإذا قلت في الشخصية: زيد حيوان فعكسه: بعض الحيوان زيد، وإنما تنعكس الجزئية والمهملة والشخصية إلى جزئية إذا كان محمولها كليا، كما في هذه المثل، وأما إن كان المحمول جزئيا حقيقيا فتنعكس كلها شخصية نحو: هذا زيد فعكسها: زيد هذا. وكذلك قولك: الإنسان زيد وبعض الإنسان زيد، فعكسها: زيد إنسان، وهو ظاهر. قوله: (لأنهم لا يعتبرون من الأحكام إلا ما كان مطردا في جميع المواد) أي: وأما قول ابن هارون: إنه عكس؛ لاطراده في باب مساواة الطرفين كاطراد الجزئية عكسا عند عدم المساواة وهم؛ لأن المعتبر من الاطراد ما لا نظر معه إلى المواد، لا مطلق الاطراد. قوله: (فعكسها كلها بالمستوي جزئية) قد نبه على هذا صاحب القادرية فقال: فالموجبات عكسها عكس استوا جزئية موجبة على سوا لكن ظاهره الإطلاق وليس كذلك، بل محل ذلك: ما إذا كان المحمول كليا، كما نبه على ذلك الشارح، ولقد أحسن الكاتبي في قوله في الموجبات: حكمها أن لا تنعكس كلية، فصدق كلامه بالجزئية والشخصية، وبيان لزوم صدق الجزئية الموجبة في عكس الموجبات الأربع: أن محمولها لما صدق في الأصل على ما صدق عليه العنوان لزم من ذلك أيضا أن عنوانها أيضا صدق على ما صدق عليه المحمول، فصح أن يجعل المحمول عنوانا في العكس لتلك الأفراد التي كان صدق عليها في الأصل، وأن يجعل العنوان محمولا على الأفراد التي دل في الأصل على صدقه عليه
Chapter on simple conversion except the universal affirmative... etc., and what Ibn Harun chose of the detail concerning it: i.e., that it converts as itself if the predicate is co-extensive, and converts as particular if it is more general — this contradicts the majority because they only consider as rulings what is regular in all materials. And the apparent meaning of the author is that all propositions convert as themselves except the universal affirmative, but it is not absolute, for you know that the primary propositions are four: universal, particular, indefinite, and individual; and each is affirmative and negative, so the total is eight. As for the four affirmatives, their simple converse is all particular affirmative. The universal is as previously; if you say in the particular: 'Some white is an animal' its converse is 'Some animal is white'; if you say in the indefinite: 'The body is an animal' its converse is 'Some animal is a body'; if you say in the individual: 'Zayd is an animal' its converse is 'Some animal is Zayd'. The particular, indefinite, and individual only convert to particular if their predicate is universal, as in these examples. But if the predicate is a real particular, then all convert to individual, like: 'This is Zayd' its converse is 'Zayd is this'. Similarly your saying: 'Human is Zayd' and 'Some human is Zayd', their converse is 'Zayd is human', and it is clear. His saying: (because they only consider as rulings what is regular in all materials) i.e., as for Ibn Harun's saying that it is a converse because of its regularity in the case of co-extensive terms like the regularity of particular as a converse when not co-extensive — it is a delusion, because what is considered of regularity is that which does not look to materials, not absolute regularity. His saying: (their simple converse is all particular affirmative) the author of al-Qadiriyya alerted to this, saying: 'The affirmatives, their simple converse is a particular affirmative equally' but its apparent meaning is absolute and it is not so; rather, that is applicable when the predicate is universal, as the commentator alerted. And al-Katibi did well in his statement regarding affirmatives: 'Their ruling is that they do not convert universally', so his statement is verified by particular and individual. And the explanation of the necessary truth of the particular affirmative in the converse of the four affirmatives: since their predicate in the original was true of what the subject was true of, it follows also that their subject is true of what the predicate was true of, so it is valid to make the predicate the subject in the converse for those individuals on which it was true in the original, and to make the subject the predicate for the individuals indicated in the original as true of it.
Source pages p. 653

Commentary
This passage defines the conversion rules for the eight types of categorical propositions, with a focus on the universal affirmative exception and a refutation of Ibn Harun's view. The commentary begins by noting that the universal affirmative does not convert as itself, except when the predicate is co-extensive — but the majority reject this exception because they require the converse to hold regularly across all materials, not just in specific cases. The author's apparent claim that all propositions except the universal affirmative convert as themselves is not absolute, because there are four primary types: universal, particular, indefinite, and individual, each affirmative and negative, totaling eight. For the four affirmatives, their simple converse is always a particular affirmative. Examples illustrate: 'Every human is an animal' converts to 'Some animal is human'; 'Some white is an animal' converts to 'Some animal is white'; 'The body is an animal' (indefinite) converts to 'Some animal is body'; 'Zayd is an animal' (individual) converts to 'Some animal is Zayd'. However, this holds only if the predicate is a universal term. If the predicate is a real particular (e.g., proper name or individual), the converse becomes individual: 'This is Zayd' becomes 'Zayd is this'. Similarly, 'Human is Zayd' and 'Some human is Zayd' both convert to 'Zayd is human'. The commentary then refutes Ibn Harun, who argued that universal affirmative can convert as itself in co-extensive cases due to regularity there. The response is that the relevant regularity must be independent of material content. Finally, the passage explains why all affirmatives convert to particular affirmative: because the predicate in the original was true of the subject's instances, so by switching, a particular affirmative follows — the subject's instances become a subset of the predicate's instances.

Eight Propositions and Their Conversion Rules
Classifies eight propositions and details conversion rules for affirmatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the eight types of categorical propositions and state the conversion rule for each affirmative type, including the condition on the predicate.

Core Idea
All affirmative propositions convert to particular affirmative if the predicate is universal; otherwise to individual.

Categorical propositions come in four primary types: universal, particular, indefinite, and individual. Each is either affirmative or negative, making eight in total. For the four affirmatives, their simple converse is always a particular affirmative, provided the predicate is a universal term. When the predicate is a real particular (e.g., a proper name), the converse becomes individual. Let's examine the rules with clear examples from logic and Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four primary proposition types
Universal (kulliyya): 'Every S is P'. Particular (juz'iyya): 'Some S is P'. Indefinite (muhmala): 'S is P' (no quantifier). Individual (shakhsiyya): 'This S is P' (subject is a proper name).
Each type also has a negative counterpart, making eight total.
2
Learn the conversion rule for affirmatives
All four affirmative types have a particular affirmative as their simple converse. For example: Universal 'Every human is mortal' → 'Some mortal is human'. Particular 'Some human is literate' → 'Some literate is human'. Indefinite 'The body is temporal' → 'Some temporal is body'. Individual 'Zayd is a prophet' → 'Some prophet is Zayd'.
This rule holds when the predicate is a universal term.
3
Understand the condition: predicate must be universal
If the predicate is a real particular (e.g., a proper name or individual concept), the converse becomes individual. Example: 'This is Zayd' (predicate 'Zayd' is individual) converts to 'Zayd is this' (individual). Similarly, 'Human is Zayd' (indefinite with individual predicate) converts to 'Zayd is human' (individual).
This is because the predicate's extension is a single individual.
4
Apply the rule to negative propositions
The passage focuses on affirmatives, but we can infer that negatives also have conversion rules (outside the scope of this lesson). For now, note that affirmative conversion always yields a particular affirmative.
5
Explain why affirmatives convert to particular
The predicate in the original was true of the subject's instances. By swapping, the subject's instances become part of the predicate's extension, yielding a particular. Formally: if all S are P, then some P are S.
This logical justification is given by the commentator: since the predicate holds for each individual S, the subject (now predicate) holds for at least some of the original predicate.

However, this holds only if the predicate is a universal term. If the predicate is a real particular (e.g., proper name or individual), the converse becomes individual: 'This is Zayd' becomes 'Zayd is this'. Similarly, 'Human is Zayd' and 'Some human is Zayd' both convert to 'Zayd is human'. The commentary then refutes Ibn Harun, who argued that universal affirmative can convert as itself in co-extensive cases due to regularity there. The response is that the relevant regularity must be independent of material content. Finally, the passage explains why all affirmatives convert to particular affirmative: because the predicate in the original was true of the subject's instances, so by switching, a particular affirmative follows — the subject's instances become a subset of the predicate's instances.

الكلية
الجزئية
المهملة
الشخصية

Key Takeaway
All affirmative categorical propositions, regardless of quantity, convert to a particular affirmative if the predicate is a universal term; otherwise to an individual proposition.

Simple Conversion of Affirmative Propositions
Author explains the conversion rule for affirmative propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to perform simple conversion on any affirmative proposition and determine the correct quantity of the converse.

Core Idea
Every affirmative converts to a particular affirmative, provided the predicate is universal.

In this passage, the author succinctly states the key rule of simple conversion for affirmative propositions. He then demonstrates it with examples from each of the four types: universal, particular, indefinite, and singular. You will learn the rule and its crucial condition: the predicate must be a universal term. This ability to convert propositions is essential for checking the validity of many arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition
Check the quantity (universal, particular, indefinite, singular) and quality (affirmative). The rule applies only to affirmative propositions.
2
Check the predicate
Determine whether the predicate is a universal term (can apply to multiple individuals) or a genuine particular (e.g., a proper name). The main rule requires the predicate to be universal.
If the predicate is a singular term, the converse remains singular.
3
Swap subject and predicate
Exchange the subject and predicate positions. Do not change the quality (still affirmative).
4
Adjust the quantifier
Add the quantifier 'Some' (or 'بعض' in Arabic) to the subject of the converse if the predicate was universal. This yields a particular affirmative. If the predicate was singular, keep the singular form.
For universal affirmative (A), the converse is particular affirmative (I). For other affirmatives, they also become I.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في العكس المستوي
إِلَّا المُوْجَبَ الكُلِّيَّة ... إلخ»، وَمَا اخْتَارَهُ ابْنُ هَارُونَ مِنَ التَّفْصِيلِ فِيهَا ؛ أَيْ: إِنَّهَا تَنْعَكِسُ كَنَفْسِهَا إِنْ كَانَ المَحْمُولُ مُسَاوِياً ، وَتَنْعَكِسُ جُزْئِيَّةٌ إِذَا كَانَ أَعَمَّ مُخَالِفٌ لِلجُمْهُورِ ؛ لأَنَّهُمْ لا يَعْتَبِرُونَ مِنَ الأَحْكَامِ إِلَّا مَا كَانَ مُطَرِداً فِي جَمِيعِ المَوَادِّ . وَظَاهِرُ المُصَنِّفِ : أَنَّ القَضَايَا كُلَّهَا تَنْعَكِسُ كَنَفْسِهَا غَيْرُ الكُلِّيَّةِ المُوجَبَةِ، وَلَيْسَ عَلَى إِطْلَاقِهِ، فَقَدْ عَلِمْتَ أَنَّ أُصُولَ القَضَايَا أَرْبَعٌ : كُلِّيَّةٌ ، وَجُزْنِيَّةٌ، وَمُهْمَلَةٌ، وَشَخْصِيَّةٌ ؛ وَكُلُّهَا : مُوجَبَةٌ وَسَالِبَةٌ، فَالمَجْمُوعُ ثَمَانِيَةٌ .
فَأَمَّا المُوجَبَاتُ الأَرْبَعُ فَعَكْسُهَا كُلُّهَا بِالمُسْتَوِي جُزْئِيَّةٌ مُوجَبَةٌ، فَالكُلِّيَّةُ كَمَا تَقَدَّمَ، وَإِذَا قُلْتَ فِي الجُزْئِيَّةِ : بَعْضُ الأَبْيْضِ حَيَوَانٌ فَعَكْسُهُ : بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ أَبْيَضُ، وَإِذَا قُلْتَ في المُهْمَلَةِ : الجِسْمُ حَيَوَانٌ فَعَكْسُهُ : بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ جِسْمٌ، وَإِذَا قُلْتَ فِي الشَّخْصِيَّةِ : زَيْدٌ حَيَوَانٌ فَعَكْسُهُ : بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ زَيْدٌ، وَإِنَّمَا تَنْعَكِسُ الجُزْئِيَّةُ وَالمُهْمَلَةُ وَالشَّخْصِيَّةُ إِلَى جُزْئِيَّةٍ إِذَا كَانَ مَحْمُولُهَا كُلِّيَّا، كَمَا فِي هَذِهِ المَثْلِ، وَأَمَّا إِنْ كَانَ المَحْمُولُ جُزْنِيًّا حَقِيقِيًّا فَتَنْعَكِسُ كُلُّهَا شَخْصِيَّةٌ ، نَحْوُ : هَذَا زَيْدٌ» فَعَكْسُهَا : زَيْدٌ هَذَا». وَكَذَلِكَ قَوْلُكَ : الإِنْسَانُ زَيْدٌ وَ بَعْضُ الإِنْسَانِ زَيْدٌ، فَعَكْسُهَا: «زَيْدٌ إِنْسَانٌ»، وَهُوَ ظَاهِرٌ .
قوله : (لِأَنَّهُمْ لَا يَعْتَبِرُونَ مِنَ الأَحْكَامِ إِلَّا مَا كَانَ مُطَرِداً فِي جَمِيعِ المَوَادٌ) أي: وأما قول ابن هارون: «إنَّه عكس؛ لاطراده في باب مساواة الطرفين كـ اطراد الجزئية عكساً عند عدم المساوة وهم ؛ لأنَّ المعتبر مِنَ الاطراد ما لا نظر معه إلى المواد، لا مطلق الاطراد. قوله : (فَعَكْسُهَا كُلُّهَا بِالمُسْتَوِي جُزْئِيَّةٌ) قد نبَّه على هذا صاحب القادرية» فقال: فَالمُوْجَبَاتُ عَكْسُهَا عَكْسُ اسْتِوَا جُزنِيَّةٌ مُوجَبَةٌ عَلَى سَوَا لكن ظاهره الإطلاق، وليس كذلك، بل محلُّ ذلك : ما إذا كان المحمول كليا، كما نبه على ذلك الشارح، ولقد أحسن الكاتبيُّ في قوله في الموجبات: حكمها أن لا تنعكس كلّيَّة»، فصدق كلامه بالجزئيّة والشَّخصيَّة، وبيانُ لزوم صدق الجزئية الموجبة في عكس الموجبات الأربع: أنَّ محمولها لمَّا صدق في الأصل على ما صدق عليه العنوان لزم من ذلك أيضاً أنَّ عنوانها أيضاً صدق على ما صدق عليه المحمول، فصح أن يجعل المحمول عنواناً في العكس لتلك الأفراد التي كان صدق عليها في الأصل، وأن يجعل العنوان محمولاً على الأفراد التي دلّ في الأصل على صدقه عليه .
Chapter on Simple Conversion.
Except the universal affirmative... etc. And what Ibn Harun chose of detail in it, i.e., that it converts as itself if the predicate is coextensive, and converts as particular if it is more general, is contrary to the majority; because they do not consider from the rulings except what is consistent in all matters. And the apparent [meaning] of the author is that all propositions convert as themselves except the universal affirmative, and it is not unrestricted, for you have known that the root propositions are four: universal, particular, indefinite, and singular; and all of them are affirmative and negative, so the total is eight.
As for the four affirmatives, their conversion by the simple [conversion] is all particular affirmative. The universal as previously; and if you say in the particular: 'Some white is an animal', its converse: 'Some animal is white'; and if you say in the indefinite: 'The body is an animal', its converse: 'Some animal is a body'; and if you say in the singular: 'Zayd is an animal', its converse: 'Some animal is Zayd'. And indeed the particular, indefinite, and singular convert to particular only if their predicate is universal, as in these examples. But if the predicate is truly particular, they all convert to singular, like: 'This is Zayd' its converse: 'Zayd is this'. And similarly your saying: 'The human is Zayd' and 'Some human is Zayd', their converse: 'Zayd is a human', and it is apparent.
His saying: 'Because they do not consider from the rulings except what is consistent in all matters' means: As for Ibn Harun's saying: 'It is conversion due to its consistency in the case of equality of the two extremes like the consistency of the particular as conversion when there is no equality', it is a delusion; because what is considered from consistency is that which has no regard to matters, not absolute consistency. His saying: 'So their conversion all by simple [conversion] is a particular affirmative' – the author of al-Qadiriyya pointed to this and said: 'The affirmatives, their conversion by simple [conversion] is a particular affirmative in equality.' But its apparent is unrestricted, and it is not so; rather its place is when the predicate is universal, as the commentator alerted. And al-Katibi excelled in his saying regarding affirmatives: 'Their ruling is that they do not convert universally,' so his statement is verified by the particular and the singular. And the demonstration of the necessity of the truth of the particular affirmative in the conversion of the four affirmatives: that when their predicate is true in the original of what the subject is true of, it follows from that also that their subject is also true of what the predicate is true of, so it is valid to make the predicate a subject in the conversion for those individuals of which it was true in the original, and to make the subject a predicate of the individuals which the original indicated its truth upon.
Source pages p. 653

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory section on the conversion (al-ʿaks al-mustawī) of affirmative propositions. It begins by referencing the well-known exception of the universal affirmative (A) and the detailed view of Ibn Harun, who distinguishes between cases where the predicate is coextensive or more general. The author notes that the majority reject Ibn Harun's view because they require the conversion rule to be consistent across all subject matters. The author then clarifies that the apparent meaning of the matn (the original text) is that all propositions convert identically except the universal affirmative, but this is not unrestricted; rather, the types of propositions are four in quantity and two in quality, making eight total.

The core rule is then stated: all four affirmative propositions—universal, particular, indefinite, and singular—convert to a particular affirmative via simple conversion. Examples are provided: 'Some white is an animal' converts to 'Some animal is white' (particular); 'The body is an animal' (indefinite) converts to 'Some animal is a body'; 'Zayd is an animal' (singular) converts to 'Some animal is Zayd'. The author adds an important condition: this rule holds only if the predicate is a universal. If the predicate is a genuine particular (e.g., 'This is Zayd'), the converse is also singular ('Zayd is this'). Similarly, if the subject is a singular term, the converse remains singular.

The commentary then addresses two points. First, it refutes Ibn Harun's argument about consistency, stating that true consistency does not depend on particular subject matters. Second, it explains the statement 'Their conversion by simple conversion is a particular affirmative' from the sharh (commentary). The commentator (presumably Bajuri) notes that this was clarified by the author of al-Qadiriyya, who restricted the rule to cases where the predicate is universal. Al-Katibi's statement that affirmatives do not convert universally is also praised, as it aligns with the particular or singular result. Finally, a demonstration is given: since the predicate is true of the same individuals as the subject in the original, swapping subject and predicate yields a true particular proposition; if the predicate were not a universal, the quantifier would not apply, and only a singular converse would result.

الموجبات الأربع

Conversion of Negative Propositions (Sawalib Arbac)
Author rules on conversion of four negatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct simple converse for any negative proposition (universal, particular, indefinite, singular) based on the logical type of its predicate.

Core Idea
Conversion of negatives depends on the quantity of the predicate, not just the subject.

This passage from al-Bajuri's commentary on al-Sullam al-Munawraq details the rules for converting negative propositions. Understanding these rules is essential for valid immediate inference in categorical logic, which forms the basis for more complex syllogisms used in kalam and fiqh. We will work through the four types of negative propositions and their conversion behavior.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four negative propositions
Know that the 'four negatives' are universal (E), particular (O), indefinite, and singular. The passage states that particular and indefinite have no conversion.
2
Determine the type of predicate
For a universal negative (E), check if the predicate is universal or particular (singular). This determines the converse form.
3
Apply the conversion rule for universal negative
If predicate is universal, converse is identical universal negative. If predicate is particular (singular), converse becomes a singular negative.
Example: 'No horse is Zayd' becomes 'Zayd is not a horse'.
4
Apply conversion rule for singular negative
If predicate is particular (singular), converse is identical singular. If predicate is universal, converse becomes a universal negative.
Example: 'Zayd is not a horse' becomes 'No horse is Zayd'.
5
Verify using examples
Practise with the given examples: 'No human is a horse' → 'No horse is a human'; 'No horse is Zayd' → 'Zayd is not a horse'; etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني على السلم المرونق
وَأَمَّا السَّوَالِبُ الأَرْبَعُ : فَالجُزْئِيَّةُ وَالمُهْمَلَةُ لَا عَكْسَ لَهُمَا كَمَا يَأْتِي فِي قَوْلِهِ : وَالعَكْسُ لَازِمٌ لِغَيْرِ مَا وُجِدْ ... إلخ»، وَالكُلِّيَّةُ إِنْ كَانَ مَحْمُولُهَا كُلِّيَّا فَتَنْعَكِسُ كَنَفْسِهَا ؛ نَحْوُ : «لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِفَرَسٍ عَكْسُهُ : لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الفَرَسِ بِإِنْسَانٍ»، وَإِنْ كَانَ مَحْمُولُهَا جُزْئِيًّا ، نَحْوُ : «لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الفَرَسِ بِزَيْدٍ فَعَكْسُهَا شَخْصِيَّةٌ وَهِيَ : «لَيْسَ زَيْدٌ بِفَرَسٍ» ، وَالشَّخْصِيَّةُ إِذَا كَانَ مَحْمُولُهَا جُزْيْيًّا؛ نَحْوُ : «لَيْسَ زَيْدٌ هَذَا» فَانْعِكَاسُهَا كَنَفْسِهَا نَحْوُ : «لَيْسَ هَذَا زَيْداً، ظَاهِرٌ، وَإِذَا كَانَ مَحْمُولُهَا كُلِّيَّا ؛ نَحْوُ : «لَيْسَ زَيْدٌ بِفَرَسٍ» فَإِنَّهَا تَنْعَكِسُ كُلِّيَّةٌ سَالِبَةً وَهِيَ : لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الفَرَسِ بِزَيْدِ ، لَا شَخْصِيَّةٌ وَذَلِكَ وَاضِحٌ .
The second [section] on al-Sullam al-Munawraq.
As for the four negatives: the particular and the indefinite have no conversion, as will come in his saying: 'And conversion is necessary for other than what exists... etc.' And the universal, if its predicate is universal, it converts as itself; e.g., 'Nothing of the human is a horse' its converse: 'Nothing of the horse is a human'. And if its predicate is particular, e.g., 'Nothing of the horse is Zayd', its converse is singular, which is: 'Zayd is not a horse'. And the singular, if its predicate is particular; e.g., 'Zayd is not this', its conversion is as itself, e.g., 'This is not Zayd', apparent. And if its predicate is universal; e.g., 'Zayd is not a horse', then it converts to a universal negative, which is: 'Nothing of the horse is Zayd', not a singular, and that is clear.
Source pages p. 654

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition of the conversion rules for negative propositions. It begins with a heading indicating it is the second part of the commentary on al-Sullam al-Munawraq. The author states that among the four negatives (particular, indefinite, universal, singular), the particular and indefinite have no valid converse. The universal negative (E) converts identically when its predicate is also universal: e.g., 'No human is a horse' becomes 'No horse is a human'. If the universal negative has a particular predicate (i.e., a singular term), it converts to a singular negative: e.g., 'No horse is Zayd' becomes 'Zayd is not a horse'. For the singular negative, if its predicate is particular (also singular), it converts identically: 'Zayd is not this' becomes 'This is not Zayd'. If the singular negative has a universal predicate, it converts to a universal negative: 'Zayd is not a horse' becomes 'No horse is Zayd'. The author notes that this last case does not yield a singular converse, which is clear from the logic of the conversion rule.

السَّوَالِبُ الأَرْبَعُ
الكلية السالبة
الجزئية
المهملة
الشخصية

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Some devil is not an angel
C) Every devil is not an angel
D) No angel is a devil (same)

Key Takeaway
The converse of a negative proposition depends on the quantity of its predicate: universal negatives with universal predicate convert identically, while those with particular predicate become singular; singular negatives with particular predicate convert identically, while those with universal predicate become universal.

Critique of the Poet's Condition on Conversion Quantity
Author critiques the poet's condition on conversion quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct conversion rules for singular propositions and critique an overgeneralized condition.

Core Idea
A poet's blanket rule on quantity in conversion fails when faced with singular propositions.

In this lesson, we examine a critical refinement in the logic of immediate inference. The poet (al-nāẓim) claimed that in conversion, quantity is preserved except for universal affirmative. The commentator shows this is not always true, especially for singular propositions. We'll explore the exceptions and understand the reasoning behind the correct conversion rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the poet's condition
The poet stated that in conversion, the quantity (universal/particular/singular) is preserved for all propositions except the universal affirmative. This is the claim being critiqued.
2
Understand the excuse for affirmative singular
Some excusers argued that affirmative singular is included in its universality (i.e., treated as universal) because it functions as a major premise in the first figure. The author rejects this as insufficient.
The excuse is not a deliverance because it ignores the actual conversion rules.
3
Analyze the negative singular excuse
They claimed that the negative singular's converse is 'like itself' universally. The author clarifies that this means identical in judgment (both universal negative), not in nomenclature (the quantifier differs).
The objector misunderstood 'like itself' as identical in form.
4
Learn the author's detailed rules
Affirmative singular: if predicate is universal, converts to particular; if predicate is particular, converts to singular. Negative singular: if predicate is universal, converts to universal negative; if predicate is particular, converts to singular.
These rules are derived from the co-truth principle and the behavior of quantifiers.
5
Summarize the underlying principle
For affirmative propositions, the co-truth of subject and predicate ensures that the converse is at least particular. For singulars, the quantifier changes based on the predicate's universality.

وَقَدْ عَلِمْتَ بِمَا ذَكَرَ أَنَّ إِطْلَاقَ النَّاظِمِ شَرْطَ بَقَاءِ الكَم فِي غَيْرِ الكُلِّيَّةِ المُوجَبَةِ لَيْسَ بِصَوَابٍ، وَأَنَّ الاِعْتِدَارَ عَنْهُ : بِأَنَّ الشَّخْصِيَّةَ المُوجَبَةَ تَنْدَرجُ فِي كُلِّيَّتِهَا ؛ لِتَنَزُلِهَا مَنْزِلَتِهَا بِكَوْنِهَا كُبْرَى فِي الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ، وَأَنَّ الشَّخْصِيَّةَ السَّالِبَةَ لَمَّا دَلَّتْ عَلَى سَلْبٍ مَحْمُولِهَا عَمَّا صَدَقَ عَلَيْهِ مَوْضُوعُهَا صَدَقَ عَلَى عَكْسِهَا بِالكُلِّيَّةِ أَنَّهُ عَكْسٌ لَهَا كَنْفَسِهَا ، غَيْرُ مُخَلِّص ؛ مَا عَلِمْتَ مِنَ التَّفْصِيلِ فِي كُلِّ مِنَ الشَّخْصِيَّتَيْنِ، وَفِي الكُلِّيَّةِ السَّالِبَةِ وَالجُزْئِيَّةِ وَالمُهْمَلَةِ المُوجَبَتَيْنِ .
حصارة -
وحاصلُهُ : أَنَّه لما كان حكمها إيجاباً ، فقد دلت على تصادق وصف الموضوع ووصف المحمول على ذاتٍ واحدةٍ فصح أن يكون كلُّ مِنَ الوصفين عنواناً ومحمولاً ، ثمَّ لا أقل التصادق أن يكون جزئيا، وإلا حصلت المنافاة، فيكذب الأصل، والفرضُ صدقه، فلزم لا محالة صدق الجزئية في عكس الأربع إن كان المحمول كليًّا، وإلا فلا يدخل عليه السُّور إن صار موضوعاً، فلا تنعكس إلَّا شخصيَّةٌ .
في
قوله : (وَأَنَّ الاِعْتِدَارَ عَنْهُ ... إلخ) المعتذر هو قدورة والهلالي.
قوله : (وَأَنَّ الشَّخْصِيَّةَ السَّالِبَةَ) بيانُهُ : أنَّ الشَّخصيَّة السَّالبة لمَّا دلّت على سلب محمولها عمَّا صدق عليه موضوعها ؛ أي من غير أن يخرج شيءٌ مِنَ الموضوع عن هذا الحكم، صدق على عکسها بالكلِّيَّة أنَّه عكس لها كنفسها ؛ أي حيث دلّ عكسها على انتفاء محموله عما صدق عليه موضوعه من غير أن يخرج شيءٌ مِنَ الموضوع عن ذلك الحكم، فعكسها حينئذ كنفسها من حيث الحكم، لا في التّسمية ؛ لأنَّه ليس مثلها في التّسمية إذا كان محمولها كلّيًّا، فمعنى قولهم: «كنفسها» حكماً، لا تسمية، كما فهم المعترض.
And you have known from what was mentioned that the poet's unrestricted condition of preserving quantity in other than the universal affirmative is not correct, and that the excuse for it: that the affirmative singular is included in its universality because it takes the place of a major premise in the first figure, and that the negative singular, when it indicates the negation of its predicate from what its subject is true of, its converse is truly considered a conversion like itself universally, is not a deliverance; [given] what you have known of the detail in each of the two singulars, and in the universal negative and the particular and indefinite affirmatives.
Summary: Its conclusion is that since its ruling is affirmative, it indicates the co-truth of the description of the subject and the description of the predicate on one essence, so it is valid that each of the two descriptions is a subject and a predicate, then the least of the co-truth is that it be particular, otherwise contradiction occurs, so the original would be false, and the assumption is its truth, so it necessarily follows that the particular is true in the conversion of the four [affirmatives] if the predicate is universal, otherwise the quantifier does not enter if it becomes subject, so it converts only as singular.
On
His saying: (And the excuse for it... etc.) The excuser is Qudura and al-Hilali.
His saying: (And the negative singular) Its explanation: that the negative singular, when it indicates the negation of its predicate from what its subject is true of, i.e., without any individual of the subject being excluded from this judgment, it is truly said of its converse universally that it is a conversion like itself; i.e., since its converse indicates the negation of its predicate from what its subject is true of without any individual of the subject being excluded from that judgment, its converse then is like itself in terms of judgment, not in nomenclature; because it is not like it in nomenclature if its predicate is universal, so the meaning of their saying 'like itself' is in judgment, not in nomenclature, as the objector understood.
Source pages p. 654

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) on the poet's (al-nazim's) condition regarding conversion. The author argues that the poet's claim that quantity is preserved in all propositions except the universal affirmative is inaccurate. The excuse that the affirmative singular can be considered a universal because it functions as a major premise in the first figure is insufficient. Likewise, the claim that the negative singular's converse is 'like itself' universally is not a valid defense. The author insists that the detailed rules presented earlier show that singular propositions have specific conversion behaviors: affirmative singular converts to a particular when its predicate is universal, and to a singular when its predicate is particular; negative singular converts to a universal negative when its predicate is universal, and to a singular when its predicate is particular.

The author then provides a summary of the reasoning for affirmative conversions: since an affirmative proposition asserts that the subject and predicate descriptions are true of the same individuals, swapping them yields at least a particular true proposition. If the predicate is universal, the converse is particular; if the predicate is particular, the converse is singular because a quantifier cannot be used on a particular term.

Finally, the author identifies the excusers as Qudura and al-Hilali, and clarifies that when the text says the conversion is 'like itself' for a singular negative, it means in terms of the judgment (universal negativity), not in nomenclature (the quantifier differs).

الشَّخْصِيَّةُ المُوجَبَةُ
الشَّخْصِيَّةُ السَّالِبَةُ

Conversion Rules for Negative and Personal Propositions
Author explains conversion rules for negatives and personals, then critiques a poet's condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the correct converse of any negative or personal categorical proposition by applying rules based on the quantity of the predicate.

Core Idea
Conversion of negatives and personals hinges on predicate quantity, not just subject type.

In this lesson, we explore the detailed rules for converting negative and personal categorical propositions. The key insight is that the quantity of the predicate determines whether the converse retains its form or changes type. Using examples from the text, we will learn to distinguish cases and avoid common errors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
First, determine if the proposition is negative or personal, and note its predicate: universal or particular?
2
Apply universal negative rule
If the proposition is a universal negative (e.g., 'Nothing of man is a horse'), check the predicate: if universal, the converse is a universal negative ('Nothing of horse is a man'); if particular (e.g., 'Nothing of horse is Zayd'), the converse is a personal negative ('Zayd is not a horse').
3
Apply personal negative rule
If the proposition is a personal negative (e.g., 'Zayd is not a horse'), check the predicate: if particular (e.g., 'Zayd is not this'), the converse is a personal ('This is not Zayd'); if universal, the converse is a universal negative ('Nothing of horse is Zayd').
Personal negatives with universal predicate convert to universal negatives, not personals.
4
Handle affirmatives (if encountered)
For affirmative propositions, the text notes that the converse must be at least particular if the predicate is universal; otherwise it converts as a personal. This follows from the co-extensiveness of subject and predicate descriptions.
Not the main focus, but relevant for completeness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا السَّوَالِبُ الأَرْبَعُ : فَالجُزْئِيَّةُ وَالمُهْمَلَةُ لَا عَكْسَ لَهُمَا كَمَا يَأْتِي فِي قَوْلِهِ : وَالعَكْسُ لَازِمٌ لِغَيْرِ مَا وُجِدْ ... إلخ»، وَالكُلِّيَّةُ إِنْ كَانَ مَحْمُولُهَا كُلِّيَّا فَتَنْعَكِسُ كَنَفْسِهَا ؛ نَحْوُ : «لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِفَرَسٍ عَكْسُهُ : لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الفَرَسِ بِإِنْسَانٍ»، وَإِنْ كَانَ مَحْمُولُهَا جُزْئِيًّا ، نَحْوُ : «لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الفَرَسِ بِزَيْدٍ فَعَكْسُهَا شَخْصِيَّةٌ وَهِيَ : «لَيْسَ زَيْدٌ بِفَرَس [ب / ٤٢] ، وَالشَّخْصِيَّةُ إِذَا كَانَ مَحْمُولُهَا جُزْيْيًّا؛ نَحْوُ : «لَيْسَ زَيْدٌ هَذَا» فَانْعِكَاسُهَا كَنَفْسِهَا نَحْوُ : «لَيْسَ هَذَا زَيْداً، ظَاهِرٌ، وَإِذَا كَانَ مَحْمُولُهَا كُلِّيَّا ؛ نَحْوُ : «لَيْسَ زَيْدٌ بِفَرَسٍ فَإِنَّهَا تَنْعَكِسُ كُلِّيَّةٌ سَالِبَةً وَهِيَ : لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الفَرَسِ بِزَيْدِ ، لَا شَخْصِيَّةٌ وَذَلِكَ وَاضِحٌ . وَقَدْ عَلِمْتَ بِمَا ذَكَرَ أَنَّ إِطْلَاقَ النَّاظِمِ شَرْطَ بَقَاءِ الكَم فِي غَيْرِ الكُلِّيَّةِ المُوجَبَةِ لَيْسَ بِصَوَابٍ، وَأَنَّ الاِعْتِدَارَ عَنْهُ : بِأَنَّ الشَّخْصِيَّةَ المُوجَبَةَ تَنْدَرجُ فِي كُلِّيَّتِهَا ؛ لِتَنَزُلِهَا مَنْزِلَتِهَا بِكَوْنِهَا كُبْرَى فِي الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ، وَأَنَّ الشَّخْصِيَّةَ السَّالِبَةَ لَمَّا دَلَّتْ عَلَى سَلْبٍ مَحْمُولِهَا عَمَّا صَدَقَ عَلَيْهِ مَوْضُوعُهَا صَدَقَ عَلَى عَكْسِهَا بِالكُلِّيَّةِ أَنَّهُ عَكْسٌ لَهَا كَنْفَسِهَا ، غَيْرُ مُخَلِّص ؛ مَا عَلِمْتَ مِنَ التَّفْصِيلِ فِي كُلِّ مِنَ الشَّخْصِيَّتَيْنِ، وَفِي الكُلِّيَّةِ السَّالِبَةِ وَالجُزْئِيَّةِ وَالمُهْمَلَةِ المُوجَبَتَيْنِ . حصارة - وحاصلُهُ : أَنَّه لما كان حكمها إيجاباً ، فقد دلت على تصادق وصف الموضوع ووصف المحمول على ذاتٍ واحدةٍ فصح أن يكون كلُّ مِنَ الوصفين عنواناً ومحمولاً ، ثمَّ لا أقل التصادق أن يكون جزئيا، وإلا حصلت المنافاة، فيكذب الأصل، والفرضُ صدقه، فلزم لا محالة صدق الجزئية في عكس الأربع إن كان المحمول كليًّا، وإلا فلا يدخل عليه السُّور إن صار موضوعاً، فلا تنعكس إلَّا شخصيَّةٌ . في قوله : (وَأَنَّ الاِعْتِدَارَ عَنْهُ ... إلخ) المعتذر هو قدورة والهلالي. قوله : (وَأَنَّ الشَّخْصِيَّةَ السَّالِبَةَ) بيانُهُ : أنَّ الشَّخصيَّة السَّالبة لمَّا دلّت على سلب محمولها عمَّا صدق عليه موضوعها ؛ أي من غير أن يخرج شيءٌ مِنَ الموضوع عن هذا الحكم، صدق على عکسها بالكلِّيَّة أنَّه عكس لها كنفسها ؛ أي حيث دلّ عكسها على انتفاء محموله عما صدق عليه موضوعه من غير أن يخرج شيءٌ مِنَ الموضوع عن ذلك الحكم، فعكسها حينئذ كنفسها من حيث الحكم، لا في التّسمية ؛ لأنَّه ليس مثلها في التّسمية إذا كان محمولها كلّيًّا، فمعنى قولهم: «كنفسها» حكماً، لا تسمية، كما فهم المعترض.
As for the four negatives: the particular and the imprecise have no conversion, as will come in his statement: 'And conversion is necessary for other than what is found...' etc. And the universal: if its predicate is universal, it converts as itself; e.g., 'Nothing of man is a horse' its converse: 'Nothing of horse is a man.' And if its predicate is particular, e.g., 'Nothing of horse is Zayd,' then its conversion is a personal proposition, which is: 'Zayd is not a horse.' And the personal: if its predicate is particular, e.g., 'Zayd is not this,' its conversion is as itself, e.g., 'This is not Zayd' – it is clear. And if its predicate is universal, e.g., 'Zayd is not a horse,' then it converts as a universal negative, which is: 'Nothing of horse is Zayd,' not a personal proposition – that is clear. And you have learned from what was mentioned that the poet's unrestricted condition of preserving quantity in other than the universal affirmative is not correct, and that the excuse for it – that the affirmative personal proposition falls under its universal because it takes its place as a major premise in the first figure, and that the negative personal proposition, since it indicates the negation of its predicate from what its subject applies to, it is true of its converse universally that it is a conversion of it as itself – is not a deliverance, given what you have learned of the detail in each of the two personals, and in the universal negative and the particular and imprecise affirmatives. Summary: The gist of it: that since the ruling of the affirmatives is affirmation, they indicated the co-extensiveness of the description of the subject and the description of the predicate on a single essence, so it is valid that each of the two descriptions be a title and a predicate. Then the least of co-extensiveness is that it be particular, otherwise conflict would occur, making the original false, but the assumption is its truth. Hence it necessarily follows the truth of the particular in the converse of the four if the predicate is universal; otherwise the quantifier does not enter when it becomes subject, so it converts only as a personal proposition. Regarding his statement: 'And that the excuse for it... etc.' – the excuser is Qaddura and al-Hilali. His statement: 'And that the negative personal...' – its explanation: that the negative personal, since it indicates the negation of its predicate from what its subject applies to – i.e., without any part of the subject being excluded from this ruling – it is true of its converse universally that it is a conversion of it as itself; i.e., since its converse indicates the negation of its predicate from what its subject applies to without any part of the subject being excluded from that ruling, its converse then is like itself in terms of ruling, not in terms of denomination; because it is not like it in denomination when its predicate is universal. So the meaning of their statement 'as itself' is in ruling, not denomination, as the objector understood.
Source pages p. 654

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the rules of conversion ('aks) for negative and personal categorical propositions. The author first states that among the four negative propositions (universal, particular, imprecise, and personal), the particular and imprecise have no conversion. The universal negative converts as itself if its predicate is universal (e.g., 'Nothing of man is a horse' becomes 'Nothing of horse is a man'), but if the predicate is particular (e.g., 'Nothing of horse is Zayd'), it converts to a personal proposition ('Zayd is not a horse'). For personal propositions, if the predicate is particular (e.g., 'Zayd is not this'), the conversion is as itself ('This is not Zayd'). If the predicate is universal (e.g., 'Zayd is not a horse'), the conversion is a universal negative ('Nothing of horse is Zayd'), not a personal. The author then critiques the poet (الناظم) who imposed an unrestricted condition of preserving quantity for all conversions except universal affirmative. The excuse that affirmative personal falls under its universal as a major premise in the first figure, and that negative personal converts as itself universally, is insufficient because the detailed rules show that personal negatives with universal predicate convert to universal negatives, not personals. The summary clarifies that affirmative propositions indicate co-extensiveness of subject and predicate descriptions; thus the converse must be at least particular if the predicate is universal, otherwise it converts as a personal. Finally, the commentators Qaddura and al-Hilali are identified as the excusers, and the point is reiterated that the phrase 'as itself' refers to the ruling (hukm), not the denomination (tasmiya), since the converse may differ in name but preserve the logical force.

سالبة

كلية سالبة

Key Takeaway
Conversion of negatives and personals depends on the quantity of the predicate: universal negatives with universal predicate convert as themselves, but with particular predicate convert to personals; personal negatives with universal predicate convert to universal negatives.

Conversion of Conditional Propositions: Six Types and Their Rules
Author extends conversion rules from categorical to conditional conjunctive propositions and details the six types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply equipollent conversion to conjunctive conditional propositions and identify which types convert.

Core Idea
Conditional propositions convert like categoricals, but with six types instead of eight.

In the previous section, we learned the rules of equipollent conversion for categorical propositions. Now, the author extends those same rules to conjunctive conditional propositions, with a subtle adjustment: the 'makhsusa' is not treated as a separate quantity, reducing the number of types to six. In this lesson, we will walk through each type and see how conversion works, using Islamic examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six types of conjunctive conditionals
The author states there are three quantities (universal, particular, imprecise) and two qualities (affirmative, negative), making six. Note that the 'makhsusa' is not separate, so we have six instead of eight.
This differs from categoricals which have eight types including makhsusa.
2
Convert the three affirmative conditionals
All three affirmative conditionals (universal, particular, imprecise) convert to an affirmative particular conditional. For example, 'Whenever something is a man, it is an animal' converts to 'Sometimes when something is an animal, it is a man.'
The author explains that the entailment ensures the converse holds at least sometimes.
3
Convert the universal negative conditional
The universal negative conditional converts to itself. Example: 'It is never the case that if something is a man, it is a stone' converts to 'It is never the case that if something is a stone, it is a man.'
This is because universal negation of entailment is symmetric.
4
Recognize that particular and imprecise negatives do not convert
The author says these have no conversion because the consequent might be more specific. Example: 'Sometimes when something is an animal, it is a man' does not convert because its converse would be false.
Be careful: only the negative ones fail to convert; affirmative ones do convert.

Source Text
