Rules of Simple Conversion for Categorical Propositions
Author defines simple conversion and states its rules for universal affirmative and negative propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rules of simple conversion to universal affirmative and negative categorical propositions and identify which propositions lack a converse.

Core Idea
Simple conversion preserves truth and quality, but quantity changes for universal negatives.

This passage from al-Bajuri's commentary explains the rules of simple conversion (al-ʿaks al-mustawī) for categorical propositions. You will learn how to convert universal affirmatives and negatives, and why particular negatives and indefinite negatives cannot be converted. Understanding these rules is essential for valid immediate inferences in logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Determine whether the proposition is universal affirmative (A), universal negative (E), particular affirmative (I), particular negative (O), or indefinite. This determines the conversion rule.
Indefinite negatives are treated like particular negatives.
2
Apply the conversion rule for universal affirmative
A universal affirmative (A) converts to a particular affirmative (I) by simple conversion. For example, 'Every human is an animal' converts to 'Some animal is a human.'
The text also notes that the universal affirmative converts as a universal in the 'opposite' (contrapositive), but simple conversion yields a particular.
3
Apply the conversion rule for universal negative
A universal negative (E) converts to a particular affirmative (I). For example, 'No human is a horse' converts to 'Some non-horse is a human.'
The converse must preserve truth and quality; the quantity changes from universal to particular.
4
Identify propositions with no converse
Particular negatives (O) and indefinite negatives have no simple converse because they combine two defects: negation and particularity. The passage proves this by showing that a true particular negative like 'Some animal is not a human' would have a false converse.
The principle: the more general cannot be negated from the more specific.

فصل في العكس المستوي
وَمِثَالُهُ: «كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ عَكْسُهُ المُخَالِفُ: لَا شَيْءَ مِنْ غَيْرِ الحَيَوَانِ بِإِنْسَانٍ . وَقَدْ عَلِمْتَ أَنَّ الكُلِّيَّةَ المُوجَبَةَ تَنْعَكِسُ كُلِّيَّةٌ فِي المُوَافِقِ وَالمُخَالِفِ بِخِلَافِ السَّالِبَةِ الكُلِّيَّةِ، فَإِنَّهَا تَنْعَكِسُ فِيهَا جُزْئِيَّةٌ، وَاللهُ تَعَالَى أَعْلَمُ .
(۷۰) وَالعَكْسُ لَازِمٌ لِغَيْرِ مَا وُجِدْ بِهِ اجْتِمَاعُ الخِسَّتَيْنِ فَاقْتَصِدْ
(۷۱) وَمِثْلُهَا المُهْمَلَةُ السَّلْبِيَّة لِأَنَّهَا فِي قُوَّةِ الجُزْئِيِّ بَيِّنْ بِهَذَا أَنَّ الجُزْئِيَّةَ السَّالِبَةَ، وَهِيَ الَّتِي اجْتَمَعَ فِيهَا خِسَّتَانِ، وَهُمَا : السَّلْبُ والجُزْئِيَّةُ ، لَا عَكْسَ لَهَا ، وَكَذَا المُهْمَلَةُ السَّالِبَةُ؛ لِأَنَّهَا فِي قُوَّتِهَا؛ بِدَلِيلِ صِدْقِ الأَصْلِ قَوْلِكَ : بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ لَيْسَ بِإِنْسَانٍ وَالحَيَوَانُ لَيْسَ بِإِنْسَانٍ، وَكَذِبِ عَكْسِهِ في قصارة.
وأَمَّا اسْماً فَهُوَ : «قَضِيَّةٌ حَصَلَتْ بِذَلِكَ التَّبْدِيلِ».
قوله : (وَمِثَالُهُ) أي : في الحملية.
وَمِثَالُهُ في الشَّرْطِيَّةِ : كُلَّمَا كَانَ الشَّيْءُ إِنْسَاناً كَانَ حَيَوَاناً، عَكْسُهُ: «لَيْسَ الْبَتَّةَ إِذَا لَمْ يَكُنِ الشَّيْءُ حَيَوَاناً كَانَ إِنْسَاناً».
وَاحْتَرَزَ بِقَوْلِهِ: «مَعَ بَقَاءِ الصِّدْقِ» مِمَّا لَمْ يَبْقَ فِيهِ الصِّدْقُ؛ كَأَنْ تَعْكِسَ لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْإِنْسَانِ بِفَرَسٍ إلَى قَوْلِكَ: كُلُّ مَا لَيْسَ بِفَرَسٍ إِنْسَانٌ. وَبِقَوْلِهِ: «دُونَ الْكَيْفِ» مِنْ أَنْ تَعْكِسَ هَذِهِ الْكُلِّيَّةَ إلَى قَوْلِنَا: لَيْسَ كُلُّ مَا لَيْسَ بِفَرَسٍ إِنْسَاناً، فَلَا يُسَمَّى عَكْساً وَإِنْ بَقِيَ الصِّدْقُ؛ لِعَدَمِ لُزُومِ الْكَيْفِ. وَبِقَوْلِهِ: «عَلَى وَجْهِ اللُّزُومِ» مِنْ أَنْ تَعْكِسَ لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الزَّوْجِ بِفَرْدِهِ إِلَى قَوْلِكَ: كُلُّ غَيْرِ الْفَرْدِ زَوْجٌ، فَقَدْ صَدَقَ هُنَا اتِّفَاقِيًّا وَلَيْسَ لَازِماً لِصُورَةِ الْكُلِّيَّةِ السَّالِبَةِ بِدَلِيلِ كَذِبِهِ في «لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْإِنْسَانِ بِفَرَسٍ» وَقَدْ مَرَّ.
قوله : (فَإِنَّهَا تَنْعَكِسُ فِيهَا جُزْئِيَّةٌ) أي : فَعَكْسُ لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْإِنْسَانِ بِفَرَسٍ»: «بَعْضُ غَيْرِ الْفَرَسِ إِنْسَانٌ».
قول الناظم : (وَالعَكْسُ لَازِمٌ ... إلخ) : قوله : (بِدَلِيلِ صِدْقِ الأَصْلِ فِي قَوْلِكَ : بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ لَيْسَ بِإِنْسَانٍ) أي : ممَّا يَكُونُ فِيهِ المَوْضُوعُ أَعَمُّ مِنَ المَحْمُولِ أو المُقَدَّمُ أَعَمُّ مِنَ التَّالِي، فَصَدَقَ قَدْ لَا يَكُونُ إِذَا كَانَ الشَّيْءُ حَيَوَاناً كَانَ فَرَساً، وَلَا صَدَقَ فِي عَكْسِهِ: «قَدْ لَا يَكُونُ إِذَا كَانَ الشَّيْءُ [فِي قَوْلِكَ : بَعْضُ] الإِنْسَانِ لَيْسَ بِحَيَوَانِ»؛ إِذْ لَا يَصِحُ سَلْبُ الأَعَمِّ عَنْ شَيْءٍ مِنْ أَفْرَادِ الأَخَصِّ، وَإِذَا لَمْ تَصْدُقِ الجُزْئِيَّةُ السَّالِبَةُ فِي عَكْسِهَا لَمْ تَصْدُقِ الكُلِّيَّةُ السَّالِبَةُ بِالأَحْرَى؛ لأَنَّ الجُزْئِيَّةَ أَعَمُّ مِنَ الكُلِّيَّةِ، وَكَذِبُ الأَعَمِّ يُوجِبُ كَذِبَ الأَخَصِّ، وَتَخَلُّفُ الصِّدْقِ فِي مَادَّةٍ وَاحِدَةٍ يَكْفِي فِي بَيَانِ عَدَمِ الِانْعِكَاسِ، كَمَا عَلِمْتَ .
Chapter on simple conversion. And its example: 'Every human is an animal' – its opposite converse: 'Nothing from non-animal is a human.' And you have known that the universal affirmative converts as a universal in the agreeing and the opposite, unlike the universal negative, for it converts in it as a particular, and Allah knows best. (70) And conversion is necessary for other than that in which the two defects are found, so be moderate. (71) And similar to it is the indefinite negative, because it is in the power of the particular; make clear by this that the particular negative, which is that in which two defects are combined – namely negation and particularity – has no converse, and likewise the indefinite negative, because it is in its power, by the evidence of the truth of the original in your statement: 'Some animal is not a human' and 'Animal is not a human', and the falsity of its converse in 'qisara'. And as for its name, it is: 'A proposition that occurs through that substitution.' His saying: 'And its example' i.e., in the categorical. And its example in the conditional: 'Whenever something is a human, it is an animal', its converse: 'Absolutely not, if something is not an animal, it is a human.' And he restricted by his statement: 'With preservation of truth' from that in which truth is not preserved, such as if you convert 'Nothing from human is a horse' to your statement: 'Everything that is not a horse is a human.' And by his statement: 'Without the quality' from that you convert this universal to our statement: 'Not every that is not a horse is a human', so it is not called a conversion even if truth remains, due to the lack of necessity of the quality. And by his statement: 'In the mode of necessity' from that you convert 'Nothing from the even is its odd' to your statement: 'Every non-odd is even', for it is true here coincidentally, but not necessary for the form of the universal negative, by the evidence of its falsity in 'Nothing from human is a horse', and it has passed. His saying: 'For it converts in it as a particular' i.e., so the converse of 'Nothing from human is a horse' is 'Some non-horse is a human.' The statement of the versifier: 'And conversion is necessary...' etc. His saying: 'By the evidence of the truth of the original in your statement: Some animal is not a human' i.e., from that in which the subject is more general than the predicate or the antecedent is more general than the consequent, so it may be true that when something is an animal, it is a horse, but its converse is not true: 'It may not be that when something is [in your statement: Some] human is not an animal'; because negating the more general from any individual of the more specific is not valid, and when the particular negative is not true in its converse, the universal negative is a fortiori not true, because the particular is more general than the universal, and the falsity of the more general necessitates the falsity of the more specific, and the non-occurrence of truth in a single matter suffices to show the non-conversion, as you have known.
Source pages p. 659

Commentary
This passage defines simple conversion (al-ʿaks al-mustawī) and presents its rules for categorical propositions. The author begins by giving an example: 'Every human is an animal' converts by contrapositive to 'Nothing from non-animal is a human.' He then states the key rule: the universal affirmative (al-kulliyya al-mūjaba) converts as a universal in both the 'agreeing' (al-muwāfiq, i.e., simple conversion) and the 'opposite' (al-mukhālif, i.e., contrapositive). Hence, 'Every human is an animal' converts simply to 'Some animal is a human' (particular) and by contrapositive to 'Every non-animal is non-human' (universal). In contrast, the universal negative (al-sāliba al-kulliyya) converts only as a particular, e.g., 'No human is a horse' converts to 'Some non-horse is not a human' (or in simple conversion, 'Some horse is not human'? Actually the example given is 'لا شيء من الإنسان بفرس' converts to 'بعض غير الفرس إنسان' which is a particular affirmative, but the text says it converts as a particular. The author then includes a poem (lines 70-71) stating that conversion does not apply to propositions combining two defects: negation and particularity. Thus, the particular negative (al-juzʾiyya al-sāliba) and the indefinite negative (al-muhmala al-sāliba) have no converse. Evidence: the statement 'Some animal is not a human' is true, but its converse 'Some human is not an animal' is false. The commentary elaborates: the term 'conversion' is defined as a proposition resulting from swapping the subject and predicate while preserving truth and quality, and necessarily (luzūman). The author then explains that the universal negative converts to a particular affirmative, as in the example. The poem's explanation emphasizes that the indefinite negative is equivalent to the particular negative, so it also lacks a converse. The argument uses the principle that the more general (al-aʿamm) cannot be negated from every individual of the more specific (al-akhass), and that the falsity of the particular (more general) implies the falsity of the universal (more specific).

Key Terms
عكس مستوي
كلية موجبة
سالبة كلية
جزئية سالبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the simple converse of the universal negative proposition 'No human is a horse' (لا شيء من الإنسان بفرس)?
A) Some non-horse is a human (بعض غير الفرس إنسان).
B) Every non-horse is a human (كل غير الفرس إنسان).
C) Some human is not a horse (بعض الإنسان ليس بفرس).
D) No non-horse is a human (لا شيء من غير الفرس بإنسان).

Key Takeaway
Simple conversion preserves truth and quality but may change quantity; universal negatives convert to particular affirmatives, and particular negatives have no converse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Convert the universal affirmative proposition 'Every intoxicant is haram' to its simple converse. Explain the quantity and quality of the result.
?
Determine whether the proposition 'Some animal is not a human' (particular negative) has a simple converse. Justify using the rule from the passage about the two defects.

Conversion and Order: Natural vs Conventional
Author clarifies that conversion only applies to naturally ordered propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between naturally ordered and conventionally ordered propositions and determine whether a given proposition can be converted.

Core Idea
Conversion requires a natural order; swapping disjuncts changes nothing.

This passage discusses a crucial distinction in logic: not every proposition can be converted. The key is the nature of the order between its parts. Understanding this will help you correctly apply conversion as an immediate inference in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of order
From the passage, find the definitions of 'naturally ordered' (murattab bi-l-ṭabʿ) and 'conventionally ordered' (murattab bi-l-waḍʿ). Note that natural order is meaningful, while conventional order is merely verbal.
2
Examine the examples given
The author gives 'Either the number is even or it is odd' as a disjunctive. Test swapping the parts: does the meaning change? Contrast with a categorical like 'Every human is an animal' to see the difference.
3
Understand why disjunctives have no converse
The author argues that the concept of a disjunctive is mutual exclusion (taʿānud). Swapping yields the same concept, so nothing new follows. Conversion requires a different concept that follows necessarily.
Al-Qutb's alternative view is mentioned but rejected; focus on the author's reasoning.
4
Apply the rule to other propositions
Given a proposition, decide if it is naturally or conventionally ordered. For example, a conditional conjunctive like 'If it is human, it is animal' is naturally ordered. A disjunctive with two exclusive options is conventionally ordered.
Remember that the discussion is about conceptual meaning, not mere verbal expression.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ قَالَ :
(٧٢) وَالعَكْسُ فِي مُرَتَّبٍ بِالطَّبْعِ وَلَيْسَ فِي مُرَتَّبٍ بِالوَضْعِ (۷۲) يَعْنِي : أَنَّ العَكْسَ إِنَّمَا يَكُونُ فِي القَضَايَا الَّتِي التَّرْتِيبُ بَيْنَ طَرَفَيْهَا طَبِيعِيٌّ ؛ أَي : مَعْنَوِيٌّ بِحَيْثُ لَوْ أُزِيلَ ذَلِكَ التَّرْتِيبُ تَغَيَّرَ المَعْنَى ، وَذَلِكَ فِي القَضَايَا الحَمْلِيَّةِ وَالشَّرْطِيَّةِ المُتَّصِلَةِ اللُّزُومِيَّةِ كَمَا تَقَدَّمَ، بِخِلَافِ القَضِيَّةِ المُنْفَصِلَةِ ؛ نَحْوُ : «إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ العَدَدُ زَوْجاً، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ فَرْداً»، فَإِنَّ تَبْدِيلَ طَرَفَيْهَا لَا يُسَمَّى فِي العُرْفِ عَكْساً ، إِذْ لَا تَأْثِيرَ لَهُ فِي المَعْنَى [ب/ ٤٣]، وَبَحْثُ القَوْمِ غَالِباً إِنَّمَا هُوَ فِي المَعْقُولَاتِ لَا فِي المَلْفُوظَاتِ، وَتَرْتِيبُ طَرَفَيِ المُنْفَصِلَةِ إِنَّمَا هُوَ بِالوَضْع ؛ أَيْ: لَفْظِيٌّ فَقَط ، فَلَكَ أَنْ تُقَدِّمَ وَتُؤَخِّرَ مَا شِئْتَهُ مِنْهُمَا ، وَالمَعْنَى بِحَالِهِ لَا يَتَبَدَّلْ، فَقَوْلُنَا : «إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ العَدَدُ زَوْجاً ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ فَرْداً»؛ مَعْنَاهُ : أَنَّ الزَّوْجِيَّةَ وَالفَرْدِيَّةَ مُتَعَائِدَتَانِ، وَهَذَا المَعْنَى بِعَيْنِهِ هُوَ نَفْسُ المَفْهُوم، مَعَ تَقْدِيم الفَرْدِيَّةِ عَلَى الزَّوْجِيَّةِ ؛ إِذِ المَعْنَى فِيهِمَا : هَذَانِ الطَّرَفَانِ مُتَعَائِدَانِ، وَمُرَادُهُمْ بِالعَكْسِ مَا يَلْزَمُ مَفْهُومَ القَضِيَّةِ عِنْدَ التَّبْدِيلِ، وَالشَّيْءُ لَا يَلْزَمُ نَفْسَهُ، فَلِهَذَا صَرَّحُوا بِأَنَّ المُنْفَصِلَةَ لَا عَكْسَ لَهَا، وَلَيْسَ مَفْهُومُ المُنْفَصِلَةِ أَنَّ الطَّرَفَ الأَوَّلَ كَالزَّوْجِيَّةِ مُعَانِدٌ لِلثَّانِي كَالفَرْدِيَّةِ، حَتَّى يَلْزَمَ أَنْ يَكُونَ مَفْهُومُهَا عِنْدَ التَّبْدِيل أَنَّ الفَرْدِيَّةَ مَثَلاً مُعَائِدَةً لِلزَّوْجِيَّةِ، وَهُوَ غَيْرُ المَفْهُومِ الأَوَّلِ لَكِنَّهُمَا مُتَلَازِمَانِ، كَمَا زَعَمَ القُطْبُ فِي شَرْحِ الشَّمْسِيَّةِ ، بَلْ مَعْنَاهَا مَا ذَكَرْنَاهُ أَوَّلاً،
Then he said: (72) And conversion is in that which is naturally ordered, and not in that which is conventionally ordered. He means: that conversion only occurs in propositions whose parts are naturally ordered, i.e., meaningfully, such that if that order were removed, the meaning would change. And that is in categorical and conditional conjunctive necessary propositions, as previously, unlike the disjunctive proposition, such as: 'Either the number is even or it is odd.' Indeed, swapping its parts is not called conversion in convention, since it has no effect on the meaning. And the investigation of the scholars is mostly in what is conceptual, not in what is verbal. And the ordering of the parts of the disjunctive is only by convention, i.e., merely verbal, so you may present and defer whichever of them you wish, and the meaning remains unchanged. So our statement: 'Either the number is even or it is odd' means that evenness and oddness are mutually exclusive. And this same meaning is identical to the concept when one presents oddness before evenness, since the meaning in both is that these two parts are mutually exclusive. And their intended meaning by 'conversion' is what follows from the concept of the proposition upon swapping, and a thing does not follow from itself. Therefore, they explicitly stated that the disjunctive has no converse. And the concept of the disjunctive is not that the first part, like evenness, is opposed to the second, like oddness, such that its concept upon swapping would be that oddness, for example, is opposed to evenness, which is other than the first concept, but they are mutual implications, as al-Qutb claimed in his commentary on al-Shamsiyya. Rather, its meaning is what we mentioned first.
Source pages p. 660

Commentary
This passage explains a key distinction: conversion (al-ʿaks) only applies to propositions whose parts are 'naturally ordered' (murattab bi-l-ṭabʿ), meaning that the order carries a conceptual significance. This includes categorical propositions (e.g., 'Every human is an animal') and conditional conjunctive necessary propositions (e.g., 'Whenever something is human, it is animal'). In these, swapping the subject and predicate (or antecedent and consequent) changes the meaning. In contrast, disjunctive propositions (al-munfaṣila) like 'Either the number is even or it is odd' are 'conventionally ordered' (murattab bi-l-waḍʿ), meaning the order is merely verbal. Swapping the two disjuncts does not alter the meaning because the proposition asserts mutual exclusion (taʿānud) between the two parts. The author refutes a claim by al-Qutb (in his commentary on al-Shamsiyya) that the disjunctive does have a converse in the sense of a mutually entailed proposition. The correct view is that the concept of a disjunctive is simply the mutual opposition of the parts; swapping them yields the same concept, not a different one. Since conversion is defined as what follows from the concept upon swapping, and a thing cannot follow from itself, the disjunctive has no converse. The passage emphasizes that the discussion is about conceptual meanings (maʿqūlāt) rather than verbal expressions (alfāẓ).

Key Terms
مرتب بالطبع
مرتب بالوضع
منفصلة
عكس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of proposition cannot be converted?
A) Categorical universal affirmative.
B) Conditional conjunctive necessary.
C) Disjunctive (e.g., 'Either the number is even or odd').
D) Categorical universal negative.

Key Takeaway
Conversion applies only to propositions where the order of parts is meaningful and changing it yields a new necessary meaning; disjunctives, being conventionally ordered, have no converse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the disjunctive proposition: 'The prayer is either valid or invalid.' Explain why this proposition cannot be converted, using the concepts of natural vs conventional order.
?

Scope of 'Aks: Natural vs. Conventional Order
Definition of 'aks: only for natural order propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish which propositions have a valid 'aks (conversion) based on whether their term order is natural or conventional.

Core Idea
Only propositions with meaningful term order can be converted; swapping mere verbal order yields no new meaning.

In this passage, the commentator explains a key restriction on conversion ('aks): it applies only where the order of terms is natural (meaningful), not conventional (merely verbal). This distinction is crucial for correctly applying immediate inference in Islamic logic. We will explore why disjunctive and accidental conjunctive propositions are excluded, and clarify a common misunderstanding about the concept of the disjunctive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of term order
The passage introduces 'ordered by nature' (murattab bi'l-tab') and 'ordered by convention' (murattab bi'l-wad'). Natural order means the meaning changes if the terms are swapped; conventional order means swapping does not affect meaning.
Example of natural order: 'Every human is an animal' – swapping to 'Every animal is human' changes truth. Example of conventional order: 'Either even or odd' – swapping does not change the mutual exclusivity.
2
Recognize propositions with natural order
The author states that categorical and conditional conjunctive necessary propositions have natural order. These are the ones where conversion is technically allowed.
Conditional conjunctive necessary: e.g., 'If it is human, it is animal' – swapping to 'If it is animal, it is human' is not valid conversion because the relation is not symmetric.
3
Recognize propositions excluded from 'aks
Disjunctive propositions (e.g., 'Either the number is even or odd') and accidental conjunctives (e.g., 'If it is a crow, it is black') are excluded because their order is conventional. Swapping terms does not change the meaning.
The author refutes al-Qutb's view that the disjunctive's concept changes upon swapping; rather, the meaning (mutual exclusivity) is symmetric and identical.
4
Apply the criterion to a new example
Given any proposition, ask: Does swapping the terms alter the meaning? If yes, it has natural order and conversion may be considered. If no, it has conventional order and is not subject to 'aks.
This criterion is independent of truth – even if the converted proposition happens to be true, if the order is conventional, it is not called 'aks.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فِي قَوْلِكَ : بَعْضُ الإِنْسَانِ لَيْسَ بِحَيَوَانٍ»؛ إِذْ لَا يَصِحُ سَلْبُ الأَعَمِّ عَنْ شَيْءٍ مِنْ أَفْرَادِ الأَخَصِّ، وَإِذَا لَمْ تَصْدُقِ الجُزْئِيَّةُ السَّالِبَةُ فِي عَكْسِهَا لَمْ تَصْدُقِ الكُلِّيَّةُ السَّالِبَةُ بِالأَحْرَى؛ لأَنَّ الجُزْئِيَّةَ أَعَمُّ مِنَ الكُلِّيَّةِ، وَكَذِبُ الأَعَمِّ يُوجِبُ كَذِبَ الأَخَصِّ، وَتَخَلُّفُ الصِّدْقِ فِي مَادَّةٍ وَاحِدَةٍ يَكْفِي فِي بَيَانِ عَدَمِ الِانْعِكَاسِ، كَمَا عَلِمْتَ. ثُمَّ قَالَ: (٧٢) وَالعَكْسُ فِي مُرَتَّبِ بِالطَّبْعِ وَلَيْسَ فِي مُرَتَّبٍ بِالوَضْعِ. يَعْنِي: أَنَّ العَكْسَ إِنَّمَا يَكُونُ فِي القَضَايَا الَّتِي التَّرْتِيبُ بَيْنَ طَرَفَيْهَا طَبِيعِيٌّ؛ أَيْ: مَعْنَوِيٌّ بِحَيْثُ لَوْ أُزِيلَ ذَلِكَ التَّرْتِيبُ تَغَيَّرَ المَعْنَى، وَذَلِكَ فِي القَضَايَا الحَمْلِيَّةِ وَالشَّرْطِيَّةِ المُتَّصِلَةِ اللُّزُومِيَّةِ كَمَا تَقَدَّمَ، بِخِلَافِ القَضِيَّةِ المُنْفَصِلَةِ؛ نَحْوُ: «إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ العَدَدُ زَوْجاً، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ فَرْداً»، فَإِنَّ تَبْدِيلَ طَرَفَيْهَا لَا يُسَمَّى فِي العُرْفِ عَكْساً، إِذْ لَا تَأْثِيرَ لَهُ فِي المَعْنَى، وَبَحْثُ القَوْمِ غَالِباً إِنَّمَا هُوَ فِي المَعْقُولَاتِ لَا فِي المَلْفُوظَاتِ، وَتَرْتِيبُ طَرَفَيِ المُنْفَصِلَةِ إِنَّمَا هُوَ بِالوَضْعِ؛ أَيْ: لَفْظِيٌّ فَقَطْ، فَلَكَ أَنْ تُقَدِّمَ وَتُؤَخِّرَ مَا شِئْتَهُ مِنْهُمَا، وَالمَعْنَى بِحَالِهِ لَا يَتَبَدَّلْ، فَقَوْلُنَا: «إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ العَدَدُ زَوْجاً، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ فَرْداً»؛ مَعْنَاهُ: أَنَّ الزَّوْجِيَّةَ وَالفَرْدِيَّةَ مُتَعَائِدَتَانِ، وَهَذَا المَعْنَى بِعَيْنِهِ هُوَ نَفْسُ المَفْهُومِ مَعَ تَقْدِيمِ الفَرْدِيَّةِ عَلَى الزَّوْجِيَّةِ؛ إِذِ المَعْنَى فِيهِمَا: هَذَانِ الطَّرَفَانِ مُتَعَائِدَانِ، وَمُرَادُهُمْ بِالعَكْسِ مَا يَلْزَمُ مَفْهُومَ القَضِيَّةِ عِنْدَ التَّبْدِيلِ، وَالشَّيْءُ لَا يَلْزَمُ نَفْسَهُ، فَلِهَذَا صَرَّحُوا بِأَنَّ المُنْفَصِلَةَ لَا عَكْسَ لَهَا، وَلَيْسَ مَفْهُومُ المُنْفَصِلَةِ أَنَّ الطَّرَفَ الأَوَّلَ كَالزَّوْجِيَّةِ مُعَانِدٌ لِلثَّانِي كَالفَرْدِيَّةِ، حَتَّى يَلْزَمَ أَنْ يَكُونَ مَفْهُومُهَا عِنْدَ التَّبْدِيلِ أَنَّ الفَرْدِيَّةَ مَثَلاً مُعَانِدَةً لِلزَّوْجِيَّةِ، وَهُوَ غَيْرُ المَفْهُومِ الأَوَّلِ، لَكِنَّهُمَا مُتَلَازِمَانِ، كَمَا زَعَمَ القُطْبُ فِي شَرْحِ الشَّمْسِيَّةِ، بَلْ مَعْنَاهَا مَا ذَكَرْنَاهُ أَوَّلاً. كَمَا نَبَّهَ عَلَيْهِ السَّعْدُ، وَمِثْلُ المُنْفَصِلَةِ الاتِّفَاقِيَّةُ المُتَّصِلَةُ، فَإِنَّ تَبْدِيلَ طَرَفَيْهَا لَا يُسَمَّى فِي العُرْفِ عَكْساً أَيْضاً؛ لأَنَّ تَرْتِيبَهُمَا لَفْظِيٌّ، لَا مَعْنَوِيٌّ، نَبَّهَ عَلَيْهِ أَيْضاً السَّعْدُ رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى.
In your statement: 'Some human is not an animal' — because it is not valid to negate the more general from any individual of the more specific. And if the particular negative is not true in its conversion, then the universal negative is even less true, because the particular is more general than the universal, and the falsity of the more general necessitates the falsity of the more specific; and a single material instance where truth fails suffices to show non-conversion, as you know. Then he said: (72) 'And conversion is in what is ordered by nature, not in what is ordered by convention.' He means that conversion only occurs in propositions where the order between the two terms is natural, i.e., meaningful, such that if that order were removed, the meaning would change. That is in categorical and conditional conjunctive necessary propositions, as explained earlier, unlike the disjunctive proposition, e.g., 'Either the number is even or the number is odd.' For swapping its terms is conventionally not called conversion, since it has no effect on the meaning; and the investigation of the scholars is mostly about intelligible meanings, not expressions. The ordering of the two terms of the disjunctive is only conventional, i.e., purely verbal, so you may present and postpone whichever you wish, and the meaning remains unchanged. Thus our statement 'Either the number is even or the number is odd' means that evenness and oddness are mutually exclusive; and this same meaning is exactly the concept when we present oddness before evenness, because the meaning in both is: these two terms are mutually exclusive. Their intention by conversion is that which necessarily follows from the concept of the proposition upon swapping; and a thing does not necessarily follow from itself. For this reason they explicitly stated that the disjunctive has no conversion. The concept of the disjunctive is not that the first term, like evenness, is contradictory to the second, like oddness, such that its concept upon swapping would be that oddness, for example, is contradictory to evenness—which is different from the original concept—but they (the two concepts) are mutually implied, as al-Qutb claimed in his commentary on al-Shamsiyya. Rather, its meaning is what we mentioned first. As al-Sa'd pointed out. And likewise the accidental conjunctive proposition—swapping its terms is also conventionally not called conversion, because their ordering is verbal, not meaningful. Al-Sa'd also pointed this out, may Allah the Exalted have mercy on him.
Source pages p. 660

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a line from the matn stating that conversion ('aks) applies only to propositions with natural order, not conventional order. The author begins by giving a counterexample to universal negative conversion: 'Some human is not an animal' is false, so its converse 'Some animal is not human' is also false, but the universal negative 'No animal is human' is even falser. This illustrates that a single false particular suffices to show non-conversion. The main point: conversion is valid only where the order of terms is natural, i.e., the meaning changes if swapped. This holds for categorical and conditional conjunctive necessary propositions (e.g., 'If it is a man, it is an animal'). But for disjunctives like 'The number is either even or odd', swapping terms does not change the meaning; the mutual exclusivity remains. Thus, conventionally, this is not called 'aks. The author then refutes al-Qutb's view that the disjunctive's concept changes upon swapping, arguing that the original concept is simply the mutual exclusivity of the two terms, a symmetric relation. The same applies to accidental conjunctives (e.g., 'If it is a crow, it is black'), where swapping is also not considered conversion because the order is merely verbal.

Key Terms
عكس
مرتب بالطبع
مرتب بالوضع
متلازمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following propositions does NOT have a valid 'aks (conversion) according to this passage?
A) Every human is an animal.
B) If it is snow, then it is cold.
C) Either the number is even or the number is odd.
D) If it is a dog, then it is an animal.

Key Takeaway
Conversion ('aks) is technically defined only for propositions with natural order, where swapping terms alters meaning; disjunctives, accidental conjunctives, and other conventional-order propositions are excluded.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the proposition: 'If it is rain, then the ground is wet.' Is this proposition a candidate for 'aks? Why or why not? (Hint: think about whether the order is natural or conventional.)

Uniform Conversion of Universal Negatives
Explains uniform conversion of universal negatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the uniform conversion rule to universal negative propositions in syllogistic contexts.

Core Idea
Universal negatives convert to universal negatives—they stay the same.

The matn mentions that the minor term is generally placed in the first and second figures. The commentary clarifies this is because the universal negative minor premise converts uniformly. Let's understand why.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Locate the universal negative proposition in the passage. It is the minor premise in the first and second figures.
Universal negative is 'No S is P'.
2
Recall the conversion rule
Universal negative converts uniformly (al-‘aks al-mustawi) to itself: 'No S is P' becomes 'No P is S'.
3
Apply to the minor premise
In first/second figures, the minor premise is universal negative, so after conversion, the minor term becomes subject, achieving general placement.
General placement means the term is subject in a universal proposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (كَمَا فِي الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ وَالثَّانِي) أي : فإنَّ الأصغر فيهما موضوع عام الوضع بالفعل. قوله : (فَإِنَّهَا تَنْعَكِسُ كَنَفْسِهَا أي: لأنَّ السَّالبة الكلية تنعكس بالمستوي كنفسها، فيصير الأصغر في العكس عام الوضع، وأمَّا الشَّكل الثَّالث فلا ينتج كلّيَّةٌ أصلاً؛ لأنَّ الأصغر فيه محمول، موضوع، وصغراه الكلية لا تكون إلا موجبة، وهي لا تنعكس بالمستوي إِلَّا جزئيَّةٌ، فلا يتأتّى فيها عموم وضع الأصغر قطعاً .
His statement: 'as in the first and second figures' means: for the minor in them is actually subject with general placement. His statement: 'for it converts as itself' means: because the universal negative converts uniformly as itself, so the minor in the converse becomes general of placement. As for the third figure, it never produces a universal, because the minor in it is predicate, subject, and its universal minor premise is only affirmative, and it does not convert uniformly except as particular, so general placement of the minor is absolutely unattainable in it.
Source pages p. 694

Commentary
This commentary clarifies the matn's brief statement. It explains that in the first and second figures, the minor term is indeed a subject in a universal minor premise, so it has 'general placement' (عام الوضع). For the universal negative minor premise, the commentator notes that it converts uniformly (عكس مستوي) to itself, meaning a universal negative remains a universal negative after conversion. Thus, the minor term becomes generally placed in the converse of the minor premise. The commentator also points out that the third figure cannot produce a universal conclusion because its minor premise is affirmative (since a universal negative in the third figure would violate the rules of the figure), and an affirmative universal converse only to a particular. Therefore, general placement is impossible, which is why the third figure yields only particulars.

Key Terms
العكس المستوي
السالبة الكلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, which type of proposition converts uniformly (as itself)?
A) Universal affirmative
B) Universal negative
C) Particular affirmative
D) Particular negative

Key Takeaway
Universal negatives convert to universal negatives, preserving truth and quality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Why the Third Figure Never Yields Universal Conclusions
Explains the impossibility of universal conclusions in the third figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the third figure of categorical syllogism cannot produce a universal conclusion based on conversion rules.

Core Idea
Because the minor premise is affirmative and affirmative universals convert to particulars, general placement fails.

The commentary contrasts the third figure with the first two. While the first and second figures allow universal conclusions through conversion, the third figure cannot. Let's explore why.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor premise in the third figure
In the third figure, the minor term is the subject of the conclusion but predicate of the minor premise. The minor premise must be affirmative (otherwise the figure rules break).
Third figure: middle term is subject of both premises.
2
Recall conversion of universal affirmative
Universal affirmative ('All S are P') converts only to particular affirmative ('Some P are S') — not uniformly.
3
Conclude about general placement
Since the minor premise is universal affirmative, after conversion it becomes particular, so the minor term cannot be 'generally placed' (subject of a universal). Hence no universal conclusion possible.
General placement requires the term to be subject of a universal proposition after conversion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (كَمَا فِي الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ وَالثَّانِي) أي : فإنَّ الأصغر فيهما موضوع عام الوضع بالفعل. قوله : (فَإِنَّهَا تَنْعَكِسُ كَنَفْسِهَا أي: لأنَّ السَّالبة الكلية تنعكس بالمستوي كنفسها، فيصير الأصغر في العكس عام الوضع، وأمَّا الشَّكل الثَّالث فلا ينتج كلّيَّةٌ أصلاً؛ لأنَّ الأصغر فيه محمول، موضوع، وصغراه الكلية لا تكون إلا موجبة، وهي لا تنعكس بالمستوي إِلَّا جزئيَّةٌ، فلا يتأتّى فيها عموم وضع الأصغر قطعاً .
His statement: 'as in the first and second figures' means: for the minor in them is actually subject with general placement. His statement: 'for it converts as itself' means: because the universal negative converts uniformly as itself, so the minor in the converse becomes general of placement. As for the third figure, it never produces a universal, because the minor in it is predicate, subject, and its universal minor premise is only affirmative, and it does not convert uniformly except as particular, so general placement of the minor is absolutely unattainable in it.
Source pages p. 694

Commentary
This commentary clarifies the matn's brief statement. It explains that in the first and second figures, the minor term is indeed a subject in a universal minor premise, so it has 'general placement' (عام الوضع). For the universal negative minor premise, the commentator notes that it converts uniformly (عكس مستوي) to itself, meaning a universal negative remains a universal negative after conversion. Thus, the minor term becomes generally placed in the converse of the minor premise. The commentator also points out that the third figure cannot produce a universal conclusion because its minor premise is affirmative (since a universal negative in the third figure would violate the rules of the figure), and an affirmative universal converse only to a particular. Therefore, general placement is impossible, which is why the third figure yields only particulars.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The third figure is restricted to particular conclusions because its affirmative minor premise converts to a particular.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
